John Locke and the Birth of Modern Epistemology
Locke's role in formalizing epistemology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the historical context and structure of John Locke's 'Essay Concerning Human Understanding' as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Epistemology emerged as a distinct discipline with Locke's systematic organization in his Essay.

This lesson examines the common narrative that modern epistemology began with John Locke's 1690 Essay, prompted by unresolved discussions on ethics and religion. Locke's work is structured in four books, covering innate ideas, ideas, words, and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger for Locke's inquiry
The passage mentions that discussions with friends in winter 1670/1671 led Locke to realize the need to examine the principles of ethics and religion, and to determine which issues are accessible to human understanding.
2
List the four books of the Essay
The passage lists the titles: Book I refutes innate ideas, Book II deals with ideas, Book III with words, Book IV with knowledge and opinion.
Note that 'book' here refers to a major division within the Essay.
3
Analyze the passage's claim about Locke's contribution
The author states that Locke's contribution was not the invention of epistemology but its systematic organization and methodological rigor.
This sets up the subsequent comparison with earlier Muslim scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نشأة النظرية
الفصل الأول
تطوّر نظرية المعرفة
يذهب كثير من الباحثين إلى أن نظرية المعرفة لم تظهر في صورتها الراهنة والتي باتت تبحث في طبيعة المعرفة، ومصادرها وطرائقها، وإمكان الحصول عليها أو حدودها، إلا على يد الفيلسوف الإنجليزي جون لوك (ت: ١٧٠٤م وذلك عام (١٦٩٠م). وقصة ذلك أنه على إثر مناقشات دارت بينه وبعض أصدقائه شتاء (١٦٧٠ / ١٦٧١م) لم يصلوا فيها إلى حل للمسائل التي أثاروها؛ فطن لوك إلى ضرورة فحص مبادئ الأخلاق والدين، والنظر في تلك القضايا أيها يكون في متناولنا؟ وأيها يفوق إدراكنا؟ فتكونت لديه فكرة البحث في المعرفة وكتب من فوره رسالة في العقل الإنساني عزا فيها كل المعاني إلى التجربة وبعد مرور ١٩ سنة (١٦٩٠م) كتب كتابه الشهير: (مقالة في الفهم الإنساني) والكتاب مجزأ إلى أربع مقالات، أسماها لوك كتباً تناول فيها ما يلي:
1 - الكتاب الأول: وعنوانه الرد على نظرية المعاني الغريزية في المعرفة بالوراثة
الكتاب الثاني: عن الأفكار
٣- الكتاب الثالث: عن الكلمات
٤ - الكتاب الرابع عن المعرفة والرأي
والحق أن البحث في المعرفة وعناصرها ليس من إبداع (لوك) مطلقاً، وإن كان أهم ما تميز به طرحه للنظرية وفق هذا النسق المتسم بتنظيمه الفني وضبطه المنهجي. أما مباحث النظرية بصورة متفرقة فإن الأمانة العلمية والإنصاف المنهجي يقضيان بالاعتراف لعلماء المسلمين الأوائل بالسبق إلى ذلك على نحو غير منكور. وعلى سبيل المثال فقد ألف أبو بكر الباقلاني (ت: ٤٠٣ هـ) كتابه (تمهيد الأوائل وتلخيص الدلائل) وكان من أول مباحثه الكلام في حقيقة العلم ومعناه، وأقسام العلوم حيث قسمها إلى ضروري ونظري، ثم تناول مدارك العلوم أي طرق المعرفة. كما تناول أبو منصور البغدادي (ت: ٤٢٩ هـ) في كتابه (أصول الدين) الحقائق والعلوم، وعدها الأصل الأول من بين ١٥ أصلاً، فتناول حد العلم وحقيقته وإثبات العلم والحقائق، وأقسام العلوم، وأسماءها، والحواس وأقسامها، وإثبات العلوم النظرية، والخبر المتواتر طريقاً إلى العلم. وخصص فخر الدين الرازي (ت: ٦٠٦ هـ) الفصل الرابع من بداية كتاب (المحصول في علم أصول الفقه) للبحث في تحديد معنى العلم والظن كما خصص الفصل الرابع للبحث في النظر والدليل والأمارة. كما وتناول ابن تيمية (ت: ۷۲۸ هـ) أجزاء محددة من نظرية المعرفة في بعض أجزاء من كتاب درء تعارض العقل والنقل، أو موافقة صحيح المنقول لصريح المعقول.
The emergence of epistemology. Chapter One: The development of epistemology. Many researchers hold that epistemology did not appear in its current form—which examines the nature of knowledge, its sources and methods, and the possibility or limits of obtaining it—except at the hands of the English philosopher John Locke (d. 1704 CE) in the year 1690. The story is that following discussions that took place between him and some of his friends in the winter of 1670/1671 CE, in which they did not reach a solution to the issues they raised, Locke realized the necessity of examining the principles of ethics and religion, and of considering those issues: which of them are within our reach? And which exceed our comprehension? Thus the idea of researching knowledge took shape in his mind, and he immediately wrote a treatise on the human intellect in which he attributed all meanings to experience. After 19 years (1690 CE) he wrote his famous book: 'An Essay Concerning Human Understanding'. The book is divided into four essays, which Locke called books, covering the following: 1 - The first book: Its title is the refutation of the theory of innate principles in knowledge by inheritance. The second book: On Ideas. 3 - The third book: On Words. 4 - The fourth book: On Knowledge and Opinion. The truth is that research into knowledge and its elements is not entirely Locke's invention, although the most important feature of his presentation of the theory is this format characterized by its technical organization and methodological rigor. As for the topics of the theory in a scattered manner, scientific honesty and methodological fairness require acknowledging the precedence of early Muslim scholars in this in an undeniable way. For example, Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (d. 403 AH) authored his book 'Tamhid al-Awa'il wa Talkhis al-Dala'il', and among its first topics was speech about the reality of knowledge and its meaning, and the divisions of the sciences, where he divided them into necessary and theoretical. Then he dealt with the sources of the sciences, i.e., the ways of knowledge. Likewise, Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi (d. 429 AH) dealt in his book 'Usul al-Din' with realities and sciences, and counted it the first principle among 15 principles. He dealt with the definition of knowledge and its reality, the proof of knowledge and realities, the divisions of the sciences and their names, the senses and their divisions, the proof of theoretical sciences, and mutawatir report as a path to knowledge. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (d. 606 AH) devoted the fourth chapter of the beginning of his book 'al-Mahsul fi 'Ilm Usul al-Fiqh' to research on defining the meaning of knowledge and conjecture, and he also devoted the fourth chapter to research on speculation, evidence, and indicator. Likewise, Ibn Taymiyya (d. 728 AH) dealt with specific parts of epistemology in some parts of his book 'Dar' Ta'arud al-'Aql wa al-Naql', or 'Muwafaqat Sahih al-Manqul li Sarih al-Ma'qul'.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage provides a historical overview of the development of epistemology (nazariyya al-ma'rifa), explaining its modern formulation by John Locke while asserting that the core issues were already treated by earlier Muslim scholars. The author begins by stating the common view that epistemology as a distinct discipline emerged with Locke's 'Essay Concerning Human Understanding' in 1690, motivated by unresolved discussions with friends about ethics and religion. Locke's work is structured in four books: against innate ideas, on ideas, on words, and on knowledge and opinion. However, the author immediately qualifies this, noting that Locke's contribution is one of systematic organization, not invention of the subject. The remainder of the passage lists several Muslim thinkers who preceded Locke in discussing epistemological topics. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (d. 403 AH) in his 'Tamhid al-Awa'il' discussed the reality of knowledge, its divisions into necessary and theoretical, and the ways of knowledge. Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi (d. 429 AH) in 'Usul al-Din' treated knowledge as the first principle, covering its definition, proof, divisions, senses, theoretical sciences, and mutawatir report as a source. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (d. 606 AH) in 'al-Mahsul' addressed the definitions of knowledge and conjecture, as well as speculation, evidence, and indicator. Lastly, Ibn Taymiyya (d. 728 AH) engaged with parts of epistemology in his works on the harmony of reason and revelation. The passage thus argues for an Islamic precedent in epistemology, emphasizing that early Muslim scholars had already explored its fundamental questions.

Key Terms
نظرية المعرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What event prompted Locke to write his Essay Concerning Human Understanding?
A) A series of lectures at Oxford
B) Unresolved discussions with friends on ethics and religion
C) Reading of al-Baqillani's work
D) A royal commission

Key Takeaway
Locke's contribution was not inventing epistemology but organizing it systematically in his four-book Essay.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own words, summarize the four books of Locke's Essay based on the passage.
?
Explain why the author claims Locke is not the inventor of epistemology, and name one Muslim scholar mentioned later who preceded him.

Early Muslim Contributions: Al-Baqillani and Al-Baghdadi
Pioneering Muslim works on epistemology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key epistemological topics discussed by al-Baqillani in his Tamhid and al-Baghdadi in his Usul al-Din.

Core Idea
Al-Baqillani and al-Baghdadi systematically classified knowledge and its sources centuries before Locke.

This lesson focuses on the epistemological contributions of Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (d. 403 AH) and Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi (d. 429 AH). Al-Baqillani in 'Tamhid al-Awa'il' discussed the reality of knowledge, its division into necessary and theoretical, and the ways of knowing. Al-Baghdadi in 'Usul al-Din' treated knowledge as the first principle, covering its definition, proof, divisions, senses, theoretical sciences, and the role of mutawatir reports.

Guided Path
1
List the topics al-Baqillani covered
The passage states he discussed the reality of knowledge, its meaning, the divisions of sciences (necessary and theoretical), and the sources of the sciences (ways of knowledge).
2
List the topics al-Baghdadi covered
The passage states he dealt with the definition and reality of knowledge, proof of knowledge and realities, divisions of sciences, the senses and their divisions, proof of theoretical sciences, and mutawatir report as a path to knowledge.
Note that al-Baghdadi included the senses and mutawatir report among the sources of knowledge.
3
Compare their approaches
Both scholars organized their works around epistemology. Al-Baqillani emphasized the division into necessary and theoretical, while al-Baghdadi provided a more extensive list including sensory and transmitted knowledge.
This shows the depth of early Islamic epistemology.

Al-Razi and Ibn Taymiyya on Epistemology
Later Muslim epistemological contributions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the epistemological contributions of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and Ibn Taymiyya as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Al-Razi defined knowledge and conjecture in usul al-fiqh, and Ibn Taymiyya integrated epistemology with the harmony of reason and revelation.

This lesson covers the later contributions of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (d. 606 AH) and Ibn Taymiyya (d. 728 AH). Al-Razi, in his 'al-Mahsul', devoted chapters to defining knowledge and conjecture as well as to speculation, evidence, and indicator. Ibn Taymiyya addressed specific parts of epistemology in his works on the relation between reason and transmission.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Razi's epistemological topics
The passage indicates al-Razi devoted a chapter to defining 'ilm (knowledge) and 'zann (conjecture), and another to speculation (nazar), evidence (dalil), and indicator (amara).
Al-Razi's discussion is within the context of usul al-fiqh.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological engagement
The passage notes that Ibn Taymiyya dealt with 'specific parts' of epistemology in his books 'Dar' Ta'arud al-'Aql wa al-Naql' and 'Muwafaqat Sahih al-Manqul li Sarih al-Ma'qul'.
Ibn Taymiyya is known for arguing for the harmony of revelation and sound reason.
3
Understand the progression of Muslim epistemological thought
From al-Baqillani and al-Baghdadi to al-Razi and Ibn Taymiyya, Muslim scholars continuously refined the classification of knowledge and its sources, culminating in sophisticated treatments.

الفصل الأول
تطوّر نظرية المعرفة
يذهب كثير من الباحثين إلى أن نظرية المعرفة لم تظهر في صورتها الراهنة والتي باتت تبحث في طبيعة المعرفة، ومصادرها وطرائقها، وإمكان الحصول عليها أو حدودها، إلا على يد الفيلسوف الإنجليزي جون لوك (ت: ١٧٠٤م وذلك عام (١٦٩٠م). وقصة ذلك أنه على إثر مناقشات دارت بينه وبعض أصدقائه شتاء (١٦٧٠ / ١٦٧١م) لم يصلوا فيها إلى حل للمسائل التي أثاروها؛ فطن لوك إلى ضرورة فحص مبادئ الأخلاق والدين، والنظر في تلك القضايا أيها يكون في متناولنا؟ وأيها يفوق إدراكنا؟ فتكونت لديه فكرة البحث في المعرفة وكتب من فوره رسالة في العقل الإنساني عزا فيها كل المعاني إلى التجربة وبعد مرور ١٩ سنة (١٦٩٠م) كتب كتابه الشهير: (مقالة في الفهم الإنساني) والكتاب مجزأ إلى أربع مقالات، أسماها لوك كتباً تناول فيها ما يلي:

الكتاب الثاني: عن الأفكار
٣- الكتاب الثالث: عن الكلمات
٤ - الكتاب الرابع عن المعرفة والرأي
والحق أن البحث في المعرفة وعناصرها ليس من إبداع (لوك) مطلقاً، وإن كان أهم ما تميز به طرحه للنظرية وفق هذا النسق المتسم بتنظيمه الفني وضبطه المنهجي. أما مباحث النظرية بصورة متفرقة فإن الأمانة العلمية والإنصاف المنهجي يقضيان بالاعتراف لعلماء المسلمين الأوائل بالسبق إلى ذلك على نحو غير منكور. وعلى سبيل المثال فقد ألف أبو بكر الباقلاني (ت: ٤٠٣ هـ) كتابه (تمهيد الأوائل وتلخيص الدلائل) وكان من أول مباحثه الكلام في حقيقة العلم ومعناه، وأقسام العلوم حيث قسمها إلى ضروري ونظري، ثم تناول مدارك العلوم أي طرق المعرفة. كما تناول أبو منصور البغدادي (ت: ٤٢٩ هـ) في كتابه (أصول الدين) الحقائق والعلوم، وعدها الأصل الأول من بين ١٥ أصلاً، فتناول حد العلم وحقيقته وإثبات العلم والحقائق، وأقسام العلوم، وأسماءها، والحواس وأقسامها، وإثبات العلوم النظرية، والخبر المتواتر طريقاً إلى العلم. وخصص فخر الدين الرازي (ت: ٦٠٦ هـ) الفصل الرابع من بداية كتاب (المحصول في علم أصول الفقه) للبحث في تحديد معنى العلم والظن كما خصص الفصل الرابع للبحث في النظر والدليل والأمارة. كما وتناول ابن تيمية (ت: ۷۲۸ هـ) أجزاء محددة من نظرية المعرفة في بعض أجزاء من كتاب درء تعارض العقل والنقل، أو موافقة صحيح المنقول لصريح المعقول.
The emergence of epistemology. Chapter One: The development of epistemology. Many researchers hold that epistemology did not appear in its current form—which examines the nature of knowledge, its sources and methods, and the possibility or limits of obtaining it—except at the hands of the English philosopher John Locke (d. 1704 CE) in the year 1690. The story is that following discussions that took place between him and some of his friends in the winter of 1670/1671 CE, in which they did not reach a solution to the issues they raised, Locke realized the necessity of examining the principles of ethics and religion, and of considering those issues: which of them are within our reach? And which exceed our comprehension? Thus the idea of researching knowledge took shape in his mind, and he immediately wrote a treatise on the human intellect in which he attributed all meanings to experience. After 19 years (1690 CE) he wrote his famous book: 'An Essay Concerning Human Understanding'. The book is divided into four essays, which Locke called books, covering the following: 1 - The first book: Its title is the refutation of the theory of innate principles in knowledge by inheritance. The second book: On Ideas. 3 - The third book: On Words. 4 - The fourth book: On Knowledge and Opinion. The truth is that research into knowledge and its elements is not entirely Locke's invention, although the most important feature of his presentation of the theory is this format characterized by its technical organization and methodological rigor. As for the topics of the theory in a scattered manner, scientific honesty and methodological fairness require acknowledging the precedence of early Muslim scholars in this in an undeniable way. For example, Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (d. 403 AH) authored his book 'Tamhid al-Awa'il wa Talkhis al-Dala'il', and among its first topics was speech about the reality of knowledge and its meaning, and the divisions of the sciences, where he divided them into necessary and theoretical. Then he dealt with the sources of the sciences, i.e., the ways of knowledge. Likewise, Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi (d. 429 AH) dealt in his book 'Usul al-Din' with realities and sciences, and counted it the first principle among 15 principles. He dealt with the definition of knowledge and its reality, the proof of knowledge and realities, the divisions of the sciences and their names, the senses and their divisions, the proof of theoretical sciences, and mutawatir report as a path to knowledge. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (d. 606 AH) devoted the fourth chapter of the beginning of his book 'al-Mahsul fi 'Ilm Usul al-Fiqh' to research on defining the meaning of knowledge and conjecture, and he also devoted the fourth chapter to research on speculation, evidence, and indicator. Likewise, Ibn Taymiyya (d. 728 AH) dealt with specific parts of epistemology in some parts of his book 'Dar' Ta'arud al-'Aql wa al-Naql', or 'Muwafaqat Sahih al-Manqul li Sarih al-Ma'qul'.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage provides a historical overview of the development of epistemology (nazariyya al-ma'rifa), explaining its modern formulation by John Locke while asserting that the core issues were already treated by earlier Muslim scholars. The author begins by stating the common view that epistemology as a distinct discipline emerged with Locke's 'Essay Concerning Human Understanding' in 1690, motivated by unresolved discussions with friends about ethics and religion. Locke's work is structured in four books: against innate ideas, on ideas, on words, and on knowledge and opinion. However, the author immediately qualifies this, noting that Locke's contribution is one of systematic organization, not invention of the subject. The remainder of the passage lists several Muslim thinkers who preceded Locke in discussing epistemological topics. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (d. 403 AH) in his 'Tamhid al-Awa'il' discussed the reality of knowledge, its divisions into necessary and theoretical, and the ways of knowledge. Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi (d. 429 AH) in 'Usul al-Din' treated knowledge as the first principle, covering its definition, proof, divisions, senses, theoretical sciences, and mutawatir report as a source. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (d. 606 AH) in 'al-Mahsul' addressed the definitions of knowledge and conjecture, as well as speculation, evidence, and indicator. Lastly, Ibn Taymiyya (d. 728 AH) engaged with parts of epistemology in his works on the harmony of reason and revelation. The passage thus argues for an Islamic precedent in epistemology, emphasizing that early Muslim scholars had already explored its fundamental questions.

Ibn Taymiyya, al-Iji, and the Development of Islamic Epistemology
Reviews contributions of Ibn Taymiyya and al-Iji to epistemology, suggesting John Locke benefited.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key epistemological contributions of Ibn Taymiyya and al-Iji as described in the passage, and explain how they relate to the cumulative nature of knowledge in Islamic thought.

Core Idea
Islamic epistemology was systematically developed by Ibn Taymiyya and al-Iji, influencing later Western thought.

This passage presents a brief historical survey of the development of epistemology in Islamic thought, focusing on two major figures: Ibn Taymiyya and 'Adud al-Din al-Iji. Both scholars, in their works, addressed foundational questions about the definition, sources, and methods of knowledge. The passage also suggests that later Western thinkers like John Locke may have benefited from this earlier Islamic tradition, highlighting the cumulative and interconnected nature of human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two scholars and their works
Identify the two scholars mentioned: Ibn Taymiyya and al-Iji. Note the titles of their works: 'Dar' ta'arud al-'aql wa al-naql' for Ibn Taymiyya and 'al-Mawaqif' for al-Iji. These are key texts in Islamic theology and epistemology.
The passage does not give the full title of Ibn Taymiyya's work, but it is implied by the description of its content.
2
Extract each scholar's contribution to epistemology
From the passage, determine what each scholar specifically contributed. Ibn Taymiyya defined the paths of knowledge and discussed the relationship between reason and revelation. Al-Iji devoted a section of his book to defining knowledge, its divisions, and whether theoretical inquiry yields knowledge. He also questioned whether sensory perceptions qualify as knowledge.
Al-Iji's work 'al-Mawaqif' is a major text in Kalam, and its first section (mawqif) is on knowledge and nazar.
3
Analyze the author's claim about John Locke and the unity of knowledge
The author asserts that John Locke likely benefited from these earlier scholars, whether Muslim or non-Muslim. The broader point is that knowledge is cumulative and that Islamic epistemology contributed to the global development of the field. Consider what evidence the passage provides for this claim.
The claim is presented as an opinion ('as it appears') rather than a proven fact.

لمسائل العلم والنظر (۱) ، بل وحدد طرق المعرفة بصورة دقيقة وواضحة(٢). عرض هذا مع أن كتابه في جملته يتناول لب نظرية المعرفة حيث يناقش فرضية نشوب تعارض بين العقل والوحي، ويخلص إلى وحدة المعرفة في المصادر والطرائق.

Chapter One: Section One - The Development of Epistemology
... to the problems of knowledge and theoretical inquiry (1), and indeed he determined the paths of knowledge in a precise and clear manner (2). He presented this while his book in its entirety deals with the core of epistemology, as it discusses the hypothesis of the occurrence of a conflict between reason and revelation, and concludes with the unity of knowledge in sources and methods.
As for 'Adud al-Din al-Iji (d. 756 AH), he made the first stance of his book "al-Mawaqif" on theology specifically about knowledge and theoretical inquiry, and in it he addressed the definition of knowledge and its divisions, and theoretical inquiry, and whether it yields knowledge (3). He also returned to speak about knowledge and its divisions, and its opposites from ignorance, and the verification of whether the perceptions of the senses are knowledge or not? (4)
Thus, all of this confirms that John Locke – as it appears – benefited from those who preceded him, little or much, and whether those from whom he benefited were Muslims or non-Muslims, the principle of the unity of knowledge or its accumulation is an epistemic gain for every researcher.

Then it discusses al-Iji's 'al-Mawaqif' and its focus on defining knowledge and theoretical inquiry. The author then concludes that John Locke likely benefited from these earlier Muslim scholars, and that knowledge is cumulative. The passage presents a historical survey rather than a formal logical argument.

Language as a Carrier of Knowledge: Divine and Human Sources
Author divides language into divine and human sources, defining their roles in carrying knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between divine and human language based on their sources and characteristics, and explain the role of the Quran in understanding Arabic.

Core Idea
Language is not neutral: divine language is absolute and timeless, human language is contextual and limited.

In this passage, the author explores the epistemological status of language. He argues that language serves as a vessel for knowledge, but its source determines its authority and scope. Understanding this distinction is critical for how we approach revelation and human scholarship. The passage also warns against a narrow focus on linguistic text that ignores natural and contextual factors, a method the author attributes to some transmitted sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources of language
The author states that language, in terms of its source, is two types: divine and human. Locate these terms in the Arabic passage.
The terms are not standard in classical mantiq but are used here for epistemological analysis.
2
Compare their characteristics
Note that divine language is described as absolute, not subject to time or space, and carries divine messages. Human language is particular, limited by acquired human experiences, and subject to temporal and spatial contexts.
3
Understand the critique of transmitted sciences
The author criticizes the tendency to rely solely on linguistic text while ignoring the natural environment, time, space, and human nature. This leads to a disconnect between revelation and the universe.
This critique does not reject transmitted sciences but calls for a more holistic approach.
4
Connect to the prophetic method
The author cites Quranic verses showing that prophets spoke in the language of their people and that the Quran was revealed in Arabic to facilitate understanding and piety. The rhetorical power of divine language has a profound effect on hearts.
This reinforces that divine language, though expressed in human language, retains its absolute nature and guidance.

اللغة لديه هذا العلم لما تمكن من إنباء الملائكة بأسمائهم، فكانت المعلومات السابقة شرطاً أساسياً في عملية التعقل عنده (۱) . أما الدور الرئيس المنوط باللغة فيتجسد في تخليد المعاني التي يتفق عليها في كل جيل وفي كل جماعة (٢). وإذا عرفنا أن وسيلة فإن السؤال الذي يطرح نفسه هنا ما مصدر هذه اللغة إذن؟ أي ما المادة المعرفية التي تملأ هذا الوعاء اللغوي؟ بوسعنا القول: إن اللغـة مـن حيـث مصدرها :قسمان: إلهية وبشرية. أما الإلهية فمهمتها حمل الرسالات والمعارف الإلهية التي يأتي بها الرسل وتتميز بالإطلاق الذي لا يخضع لخبرات الزمان والمكان، بل يستوعب التطور ويوجهه نحو غاياتهومقاصده. وأما البشرية فمهمتها حمل الأفكار والمعارف البشرية التي يأتي بها العلماء. وتتميز بالجزئية المحدّدة بالخبرات الإنسانية المكتسبة، وتخضع لحدود الزمان والمكان الذي ولدت فيه ونشأت كما أنها محكومة بجملة الظروف الموضوعية زمانية كانت أو مكانية(۳). ومع هذه المكانة التي تحتلها اللغة في نظرية المعرفة، إلا أن الانفصال الذي قد يحصل أحياناً بين الوحي والكون يرجع - فيما يرجع – إلى اعتماد العلوم النقلية على الربط بين النص والمعجم اللغوي على نحو مفرق، مع تجاهل في كثير من الأحيان للبيئة الطبيعية، ومعطيات الزمان والمكان وطباع الإنسان، والآثار التي يحدثها كل ذلك التوجه في عملية فقه النص الشرعي والفقه المعرفي (٤). ولئن قامت جهود إحياء للغة العربية لتصبح لغة البحث والدراسة فلن تعزل القرآن الكريم من أن يكون هو ذاته آلة فهمها () بالدرجة الأولى. إن ضرورة الجمع بين كل تلك المعطيات هو منهج الأنبياء - عليهم السلام- في التخاطب مع أقوامهم وَما أَرْسَلْنا مِنْ رَسُول إلا بلسان قَوْمِهِ لِيُبَيِّنَ لَهُمْ فَيُضِلُّ اللهُ مَنْ يَشَاءُ وَيَهْدِي مَنْ يَشَاءُ وَهُوَ الْعَزِيزُ الْحَكِيمُ (إبراهيم: ٤/١٤). والرسول ما جاء بالقرآن عربياً مبيناً ، بغية أن يتعقله العرب قبل غيرهم لأنـه أنزل بلسانهم وإنا جَعَلْنَاهُ قُرْآناً عَرَبيّاً لَعَلَّكُمْ تَعْقِلُونَ ﴾ [الزخرف: ٣/٤٣) فَإِنَّما يَسَّرْنَاهُ بلِسانِكَ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَتَذَكَّرُونَ ﴾ [الدخان: ٥٨/٤٤]، ومن هنا تتحقق التقـــوى وقُرْآناً عَرَبِيَّاً غَيْرَ ذِي عِوَجِ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَتَّقُونَ ﴾ [الزمر: ٢٨/٣٩)، لأن الحجة البيانية ذات تأثير بليغ في قلوب الأسوياء من الناس ونفوسهم.
Language: He had this knowledge when he was able to inform the angels of their names, so prior information was an essential condition in the process of intellection for him (1). As for the main role entrusted to language, it is embodied in preserving the meanings agreed upon in every generation and every community (2). And if we know that it is a means, then the question that arises here is: what is the source of this language then? That is, what is the cognitive material that fills this linguistic vessel? We can say: Language, in terms of its source, is two types: divine and human. As for the divine, its mission is to carry the divine messages and knowledge brought by the messengers, and it is characterized by absoluteness that does not submit to the experiences of time and space, but rather accommodates development and directs it towards its ends and purposes. As for the human, its mission is to carry human ideas and knowledge brought by scholars, and it is characterized by particularity defined by acquired human experiences, and it is subject to the limits of time and space in which it was born and grew, and it is also governed by a set of objective circumstances, whether temporal or spatial (3). And with this status that language occupies in epistemology, the separation that may sometimes occur between revelation and the universe is due - among other things - to the reliance of transmitted sciences on linking the text to the linguistic lexicon in a dispersed manner, with frequent disregard for the natural environment, the data of time and space, human nature, and the effects that all this orientation produces in the process of understanding the legal text and cognitive jurisprudence (4). And if efforts to revive the Arabic language to become the language of research and study were made, the Holy Quran would not be isolated from being itself the instrument of its understanding ( ) in the first degree. The necessity of combining all these data is the method of the prophets - peace be upon them - in communicating with their people: 'And We did not send any messenger except with the language of his people to make clear to them, so Allah leads astray whom He wills and guides whom He wills, and He is the Exalted in Might, the Wise' (Ibrahim 14:4). And the Messenger brought the Quran as an Arabic clarification, so that the Arabs might understand it before others because it was revealed in their language: 'Indeed, We have made it an Arabic Quran that you might understand' (Az-Zukhruf 43:3). 'So We have only made it easy in your tongue that they might remember' (Ad-Dukhan 44:58), and from here piety is achieved: 'An Arabic Quran, without any crookedness, that they might become righteous' (Az-Zumar 39:28), because the rhetorical argument has a profound effect on the hearts and souls of sound people.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of language as a carrier of knowledge. The author begins by referencing Adam's knowledge of the names of things, highlighting that prior information is essential for understanding. Then he poses the question of the source of language, dividing it into two types: divine and human. Divine language is absolute, carries revelation, and is not bound by time and space; human language is particular, derived from human experience, and limited by context. The author then criticizes the tendency in transmitted sciences to focus excessively on linguistic text while ignoring natural and contextual factors, leading to a disconnect between revelation and the universe. He emphasizes that the Quran itself is the primary tool for understanding Arabic. Finally, he invokes the prophetic method of speaking in the language of their people, citing Quranic verses to show that the Quran was revealed in Arabic to facilitate understanding and piety, and that rhetorical argument has a powerful effect on hearts.

Ibn Khaldun's Binary Classification of Knowledge
Author presents Ibn Khaldun's binary classification of knowledge, then distinguishes divine science from created sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Ibn Khaldun's two types of knowledge (natural and rational positivist) and explain the role of reason in each.

Core Idea
Reason discovers natural knowledge but only derives branches from revelation.

This passage presents Ibn Khaldun's binary classification of knowledge, which is foundational for understanding the relationship between reason and revelation in Islamic epistemology. You will parse the two categories and see how reason functions differently in each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Read the passage and locate the two types: natural knowledge (ma'rifa tabi'iyya) and rational positivist knowledge (ma'rifa 'aqliyya wad'iyya). Notice that they are introduced as a binary division.
2
Determine the source of each category
For natural knowledge, the source is human thought and perception ('fikrihi wa madaarikihi al-bashariyya'). For rational positivist knowledge, the source is the report from the divine lawgiver ('al-khabar 'an al-wadi' al-shar'i').
3
Analyze the role of reason
In natural knowledge, reason independently discovers subjects, issues, proofs, and distinguishes truth from error. In rational positivist knowledge, reason has no role except in attaching subsidiary issues to principles ('illa fi ilhaq al-furu' min masailiha bi al-usul').
This limited role corresponds to qiyas (analogy) in usul al-fiqh.
4
Examine the secondary distinction
The passage then mentions divine science ('ilm ilahi') as God's eternal knowledge, described as 'neither necessary nor acquired' (laysa bi daruri wa la muktasab). This is contrasted with 'created sciences' (mentioned but not elaborated).
5
Apply the classification
Think of an Islamic science (e.g., tafsir, fiqh, kalam). Determine which category it primarily belongs to and how reason is used within it according to Ibn Khaldun's scheme.

تصنيفات المعرفة يختلف الباحثون في تصنيف المعرفة اختلافات واسعة، يرجع بعضها إلى نزعة المصنف العقدية، كما يرجع إلى منحى فني في التصنيف من حيث الإجمال والتفصيل. ولعل التقسيم التالي يقدم توضيحا لذلك: أولاً: التصنيف الثنائي: ١- التصنيف الخلدوني: الذي يعتبر المعرفة: - المعرفة الطبيعية وهي العلوم الحكمية الفلسفية، أي تلك التي يمكن للإنسان أن يقف عليها بفكره ويهتدي بمداركه البشرية إلى موضوعاتها ومسائلها، وأنحاء براهينها، ووجوه تعليمها حتى يصل إلى التمييز بعد النظر والبحث، ومنها يتبين الخطأ والصواب. - المعرفة العقلية الوضعية : المستندة إلى الخبر عن الواضع الشرعي، وهذه لا مجال للعقل فيها، إلا في إلحاق الفروع من مسائلها بالأصول (۱). - علم إلهي وعلوم محدثة: ب العلم الإلهي وهو علم الله تعالى ويتصف بأنه قديم مطلق، ليس بضروري ولا مكتسب.
Classifications of knowledge. Researchers differ widely in classifying knowledge, some due to the classifier's doctrinal inclination, and others due to a technical approach in classification in terms of generality and detail. Perhaps the following division provides clarification for that: First: The binary classification: 1- The Khaldunian classification: which considers knowledge: - Natural knowledge: which is philosophical wisdom sciences, i.e., those that man can grasp with his thought and be guided by his human perceptions to their subjects, issues, types of proofs, and methods of teaching until he reaches distinction after reflection and research, and from them error and correctness become apparent. - Rational positivist knowledge: based on the report from the divine lawgiver, and there is no place for reason in it, except in attaching subsidiary issues to the principles (1). - Divine science and created sciences: B. Divine science: which is the knowledge of Allah Almighty, and is characterized as absolutely eternal, neither necessary nor acquired.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents classifications of knowledge. The author notes that researchers disagree due to doctrinal and technical factors. Then he introduces the binary classification, specifically the Khaldunian classification. Natural knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-tabi'iyya) consists of philosophical sciences that humans can attain through reason and perception, allowing distinction between truth and error. Rational positivist knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-'aqliyya al-wad'iyya) is based on the report of the divine lawgiver (al-wadi' al-shar'i); reason only plays a role in deriving subsidiary rulings from principles. Then a second division is given: divine science ('ilm ilahi) which is God's eternal knowledge, neither necessary (daruri) nor acquired (muktasab), and created sciences ( 'ulum muhdatha) which are presumably humanly acquired. The passage ends mid-sentence after describing divine science.

معرفة طبيعية
معرفة عقلية وضعية
علم إلهي

Ibn Khaldun divides knowledge into that which reason discovers (natural) and that which is based on revelation (rational positivist), where reason only derives subsidiary issues from principles.

Tripartite Classifications of Knowledge
Author presents three tripartite classifications of knowledge and comments on the certainty criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three tripartite classifications of knowledge and evaluate the two competing definitions of certainty (correspondence vs. effort).

Core Idea
Certainty can mean correspondence to reality or sincere effort—this debate shapes Islamic epistemology.

This passage presents three different ways to classify knowledge, each using a distinct criterion. The first classifies by subject matter (natural, human, social), the second by method and stage (sensory, contemplative, empirical), and the third by the degree of certainty (certain, conjectural, false). The author then highlights a critical epistemological contrast: whether certainty is defined as correspondence of thought to reality or as the effort to seek truth, regardless of correspondence. This distinction has deep implications for Islamic sciences like Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three classifications
The passage explicitly states three tripartite schemes: (1) Hirst's classification into natural sciences, humanities, and social studies; (2) logical positivism's classification into sensory, contemplative, and empirical scientific knowledge; (3) a certainty-based classification into certain, conjectural, and false knowledge.
These are not competing but based on different criteria: subject matter, method, and certainty.
2
Analyze the criterion of each classification
For each scheme, determine the organizing principle. Hirst uses academic disciplines. Logical positivism uses the degree of systematic observation and verifiability. The third uses the truth value relative to reality.
The third classification directly ties to epistemological debates in Islamic thought.
3
Unpack the debate on certainty
The passage contrasts two views: (a) certainty (yaqin) as correspondence between thought and reality; (b) certainty as sincere effort to reach truth, irrespective of correspondence. The author notes that the third classification assumes the correspondence view, but other scholars reject that.
This debate affects how we judge the validity of knowledge in religious sciences.
4
Relate to Islamic epistemology
In Usul al-Fiqh, knowledge is often divided into qat'i (certain) and zanni (conjectural). The third classification mirrors this. The debate about effort vs. correspondence connects to whether a mujtahid's ruling is valid if his effort leads to a wrong conclusion.
This step shows the practical relevance of the passage's theoretical distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً: التصنيف الثلاثي للمعرفة: ١ - قام هيرست (Hirst) بتصنيفها على النحو التالي: أ - المعرفة الطبيعية (Natural Sciences) ب المعرفة الإنسانية (Humanities) جـ - المعرفة الاجتماعية) (Social Studies) ٢ - تصنيف الوضعية المنطقية وقد روعي فيها اعتبار المراحل حيث بدأت من: أ- المعرفة الحسية وهي ملاحظة الظواهر الكونية ملاحظة عادية، من غير إدراك للعلاقات القائمة بينها كالعلاقة بين ظاهرة بزوغ الشمس وغروبها، و سبب حدوث ذلك. ب - المعرفة التأملية: وهي عبارة عن التأمل فيما وراء الطبيعة أو الميتافيزيقيا) عن الخالق وصفاته والموت والحياة أي عن أسباب ذلك. ولما تعذر استخدام التجربة المباشرة في ذلك فقد صارت المعرفة مجرد تأمل. جـ - المعرفة العلمية التجريبية : وهي عبارة عن ملاحظة منظمة مقصودة، تقوم على أساس من الملاءمة ويمكن التحقق منها عن طريق التجربة، وجمع البيانات وتحليلها (٢). ٣- التصنيف من منطلق اليقين وثمة من يصنف المعرفة بناء على أن معيار اليقين هو مطابقة الفكر للواقع وفي ضوء ذلك تم تصنيفها إلى: أ- المعرفة اليقينية ب المعرفة الظنية ج - المعرفة الخاطئة (۳). ويلاحظ أن هذا التصنيف قائم على اعتبار المطابقة على خلاف من ذهب إلى أن اليقين المعرفي مراد به بذل الجهد في سبيل الوصول إلى الحقيقة بصرف النظر عما إذا كان مطابقاً للأمر والواقع نفسيهما، أو لعلم الله، أم لا؟ (۱).
Secondly: The tripartite classification of knowledge: 1 - Hirst classified it as follows: a - Natural Sciences, b - Humanities, c - Social Studies. 2 - The classification of logical positivism, in which stages were considered, beginning from: a - Sensory knowledge: it is the ordinary observation of cosmic phenomena, without perceiving the relationships between them, such as the relationship between the phenomenon of sunrise and sunset and the reason for its occurrence. b - Contemplative knowledge: it is contemplation of metaphysics (al-meta-physics) concerning the Creator, His attributes, death and life, i.e., about the reasons for that. And since direct experience could not be used in that, knowledge became mere contemplation. c - Empirical scientific knowledge: it is a systematic, intentional observation based on suitability and can be verified through experiment, data collection and analysis. 3 - Classification from the perspective of certainty: there are those who classify knowledge based on the criterion that certainty is the correspondence of thought to reality, and in light of that, it was classified into: a - Certain knowledge, b - Conjectural knowledge, c - False knowledge. And it is noted that this classification is based on considering correspondence, contrary to those who held that cognitive certainty means exerting effort to reach the truth regardless of whether it corresponds to the matter and reality themselves, or to God's knowledge, or not.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct tripartite classifications of knowledge, each from a different perspective. The first classification, attributed to Hirst, categorizes knowledge by subject matter into natural sciences, humanities, and social studies. The second classification, from logical positivism, categorizes knowledge by method and stages: sensory knowledge (mere observation), contemplative knowledge (metaphysical speculation), and empirical scientific knowledge (systematic experimentation). The third classification is from the perspective of certainty, where the criterion is correspondence of thought to reality, dividing knowledge into certain, conjectural, and false. The author then notes a critical point: this correspondence-based classification is not universally accepted; another view holds that certainty is defined by the effort to seek truth, regardless of correspondence, whether to reality or to God's knowledge. This comment highlights an epistemological debate in Islamic thought.

المعرفة التأملية
اليقين

Key Takeaway
The passage shows that knowledge can be classified by subject, method, or certainty, and the debate about certainty's definition has direct implications for Islamic epistemology.

The Islamic Quadripartite Classification of Knowledge
Author presents a four-level classification of knowledge based on Islamic sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the four ranks of knowledge in the Islamic quadripartite classification, citing their Quranic evidence and hierarchical order.

Core Idea
Islamic knowledge is hierarchical: faith in God is supreme, then Sharia, self-knowledge, and knowledge of the universe.

How does Islam categorize knowledge? Beyond the division into 'religious' and 'worldly', some scholars propose a quadripartite hierarchy rooted in Quranic verses. This lesson examines a four-rank classification that puts faith in God at the apex and includes self-knowledge and knowledge of the universe as valid Islamic concerns. Understanding this hierarchy helps you see how traditional Islamic epistemology integrates spiritual, legal, and cosmological knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four ranks
Locate the four levels the author lists (a, b, c, d). Notice that each is introduced with a letter and a heading.
The ranks are: faith in God (iman), Sharia rulings, self-knowledge, and knowledge of life/universe.
2
Analyze the hierarchy
The author calls the first rank 'the highest rank'. Observe the ordering: faith in God is first, then Sharia, then self, then the world. Why might divine knowledge be placed above legal knowledge?
This ranking prioritizes the source (revelation) over derived knowledge and internal reflection over external observation.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
For each rank, a Quranic verse is cited. Read the translation of each verse. Note how the verse supports that particular kind of knowledge (e.g., 'shahida Allahu...' for tawhid).
The author uses Quranic proof texts to legitimize each rank as a form of knowledge.
4
Connect to Islamic sciences
Reflect on which Islamic disciplines correspond to each rank: tawhid is the domain of kalam and aqida; Sharia rulings are usul al-fiqh and fiqh; self-knowledge relates to tazkiya and akhlaq; knowledge of the universe is the subject of cosmological reflection in tafsir and kalam.
This classification integrates multiple sciences under an Islamic umbrella.

ثالثاً: التصنيف الرباعي للمعرفة ومن منطلق إيماني شرعي خالص ذهب أحد الباحثين إلى تصنيف المعرفة وفق أربع مراتب على النحو التالي: أ- أعلى مراتب المعرفة الإيمان بالله ووحدانيته (۲)، وما يتعلق بذلك من صفات عليا، وأسماء حسنى مصدرها الله (الوحي) شَهِدَ اللهُ أَنَّهُ لا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ وَالْمَلائِكَةُ وَأُولُو الْعِلْمِ قَائِماً بِالْقِسْطِ....) Thirdly: The quadripartite classification of knowledge. From a purely Islamic faith-based perspective, one researcher classified knowledge into four ranks as follows: a - The highest rank of knowledge is faith in God and His oneness, and what pertains to that of lofty attributes and beautiful names, whose source is God (revelation): 'God bears witness that there is no deity except Him, and the angels and those of knowledge [also bear witness]...' (Qur'an 3:18). b - Knowledge of the rulings of Sharia in matters of the lawful and unlawful: 'Those who follow the Messenger, the unlettered prophet, whom they find written in what they have of the Torah and the Gospel, who enjoins upon them what is right and forbids them what is wrong and makes lawful for them the good things and prohibits for them the evil and relieves them of their burden and the shackles which were upon them' (Qur'an 7:157). c - Man's knowledge of himself: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' (Qur'an 51:21). d - Man's knowledge of life, including society and the universe: 'And on the earth are signs for the certain [in faith]' (Qur'an 51:20).
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents a quadripartite classification of knowledge from a purely Islamic perspective, attributed to a researcher cited by the author. The classification is hierarchical, with the highest rank being knowledge of God (faith in His oneness), supported by revelation. The second rank is knowledge of Sharia rulings, distinguishing between lawful and unlawful, as indicated in the Quranic verse about following the Prophet. The third rank is self-knowledge, based on the verse 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' The fourth rank is knowledge of life, including society and the universe, based on the verse 'And on the earth are signs for the certain.' This classification prioritizes divine knowledge over worldly knowledge, but includes both as part of an Islamic epistemological framework.

معرفة النفس (ma'rifat al-nafs) - self-knowledge

Islamic knowledge is hierarchically ordered: faith in God reigns supreme, followed by Sharia, self-knowledge, and knowledge of the world, all grounded in Quranic revelation.

Characteristics of Quranic Knowledge
Enumerates four characteristics of Quranic knowledge with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four characteristics of Quranic knowledge and match each to its supporting Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Quranic knowledge is complete, comprehensive, true and just, and inimitable.

In this passage, the author outlines four key characteristics that define knowledge derived from the Qur'an. Each characteristic is supported by a specific verse, illustrating how the Qur'an itself testifies to its own epistemological properties. Understanding these characteristics helps you appreciate the foundational claims Muslims make about the Qur'an's sufficiency and authority in guiding knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Extract the first characteristic: completeness (al-kamal)
Read the first bullet and the supporting verse 2:106. Notice that the verse speaks about abrogation: Allah replaces a verse with a better or similar one. This implies no deficiency in revelation.
2
Extract the second characteristic: comprehensiveness (al-shumul)
Read the second bullet and verse 6:38. The verse says 'We have not neglected in the Book anything', meaning the Qur'an covers everything necessary.
3
Extract the third characteristic: truth and justice (al-sidq wa al-'adl)
Read the third bullet and verse 6:115. The verse states that Allah's word is fulfilled in truth and justice, unchangeable.
4
Extract the fourth characteristic: inimitability (al-i'jaz)
Read the fourth bullet with two verses (11:13 and 17:88). The challenge is to produce something like the Qur'an; the claim is that it is impossible even with combined efforts.
5
Summarize the mapping
Create a mental table: completeness ↔ 2:106, comprehensiveness ↔ 6:38, truth/justice ↔ 6:115, inimitability ↔ 11:13 & 17:88.

سمات المعرفة
تتسم المعرفة القرآنية – باعتبارها انعكاساً لخصائص القرآن الكريم - بجملة خصائص منها:
1 - الكمال: ﴿مَا نَنسَخْ مِنْ آيَةٍ أَوْ نُنسِهَا نَأْتِ بِخَيْرٍ مِنْهَا أَوْ مِثْلِهَا أَلَمْ تَعْلَمْ أَنَّ اللَّهَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ﴾ [البقرة: ١٠٦/٢].
٢ - الشمول: ﴿مَا فَرَّطْنَا فِي الْكِتابِ مِنْ شَيْءٍ﴾ [الأنعام: ٣٨/٦].
٣ - الصدق والعدل: ﴿وَتَمَّتْ كَلِمَةُ رَبِّكَ صِدْقاً وَعَدْلاً لا مُبَدِّلَ لِكَلِمَاتِهِ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْعَلِيمُ﴾ [الأنعام: ١١٥/٦].
٤ - الإعجاز: ﴿أَمْ يَقُولُونَ افْتَراهُ قُلْ فَأْتُوا بِعَشْرِ سُوَرٍ مِثْلِهِ مُفْتَرَيَاتٍ وَادْعُوا مَنِ اسْتَطَعْتُمْ مِنْ دُونِ اللَّهِ إِن كُنتُمْ صادِقِينَ﴾ [هود: ۱۳/۱۱]، ﴿قُلْ لَئِنِ اجْتَمَعَتِ الإِنسُ وَالْجِنُّ عَلَى أَنْ يَأْتُوا بِمِثْلِ هَذَا الْقُرْآنِ لَا يَأْتُونَ بِمِثْلِهِ وَلَوْ كَان بَعْضُهُمْ لِبَعْضٍ ظَهِيراً﴾ [الإسراء: ۸۸/۱۷].
Characteristics of knowledge
Quranic knowledge – as a reflection of the characteristics of the Noble Quran – is marked by a number of characteristics, including:
1 - Completeness: {Whatever verse We abrogate or cause to be forgotten, We bring one better than it or similar to it. Do you not know that Allah is over all things competent?} [Al-Baqarah 2:106].
2 - Comprehensiveness: {We have not neglected in the Book anything} [Al-An'am 6:38].
3 - Truth and justice: {And the word of your Lord has been fulfilled in truth and justice. None can alter His words, and He is the Hearing, the Knowing} [Al-An'am 6:115].
4 - Inimitability: {Or do they say, 'He invented it'? Say, 'Then bring ten surahs like it, invented, and call upon whoever you can besides Allah, if you should be truthful'} [Hud 11:13]; {Say, 'If mankind and jinn gathered together to produce the like of this Quran, they could not produce the like of it, even if they were to each other assistants'} [Al-Isra 17:88].
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines four characteristics of Qur'anic knowledge, each supported by a Qur'anic verse. The author treats these as inherent properties of the Qur'an itself, reflected in the knowledge derived from it. The first characteristic is completeness (al-kamal), citing verse 2:106 which indicates that whenever a verse is abrogated, Allah brings a better or similar one, implying no deficiency in divine revelation. The second is comprehensiveness (al-shumul), from 6:38, asserting that the Book neglects nothing. The third is truth and justice (al-sidq wa al-'adl), from 6:115, affirming that Allah's word is intrinsically true and just, immune to alteration. The fourth is inimitability (al-i'jaz), supported by two challenges: to produce ten surahs like it (11:13) and to produce its like even with combined human and jinn efforts (17:88). Thus, these verses serve as evidence that Qur'anic knowledge is perfect, all-encompassing, truthful, just, and miraculous. The author's method is to enumerate and then illustrate each point with scripture, making this a definitional expansion of the concept of Qur'anic epistemology.

Key Terms
الكمال
الشمول
الصدق والعدل
الإعجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 6:38
C) 6:115
D) 11:13

Key Takeaway
Quranic knowledge is characterized by completeness, comprehensiveness, truth/justice, and inimitability, each explicitly supported by Qur'anic verses.

Quranic Epistemological Characteristics and Their Proofs
Lists characteristics of Quran as source of knowledge with supporting verses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and match each Quranic epistemological characteristic with its supporting verse from the passage.

Core Idea
The Quran's epistemological authority is demonstrated through distinct characteristics, each proven by specific verses.

This passage from a contemporary work on Islamic epistemology enumerates six key characteristics of the Quran that establish its role as a source of knowledge. Each characteristic is not merely asserted but grounded in a direct Qur'anic proof. Your task is to learn these characteristics and understand how they function as epistemological pillars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six characteristics
Scan the Arabic text and find the terms listed: الفاعلية الحضارية, الحفظ, العالمية, اليسر, البيان, الانفتاح المعرفي. Note that they are listed with numbers or bullet points.
Only six are visible in this excerpt; the passage may have originally had ten.
2
Extract the supporting verse for each
For each characteristic, locate the accompanying Qur'anic verse. For example, for الحفظ, the verses are 26:210-212 and 15:9. Write down the characteristic-verse pairs.
Some verses are cited with surah and ayah numbers; verify the text.
3
Understand the logical link
Consider why each verse supports the characteristic. For الحفظ, the verse declares Allah as the guardian of the Quran, directly implying preservation. For البيان, verses call the Quran 'clear verses' and a 'clear Book'.
The connection is explicit in some cases, implicit in others (e.g., civilizational effectiveness from jihad with the Quran).
4
Recognize the epistemological function
These characteristics together argue that the Quran is a reliable, universal, accessible, and open source of knowledge. They respond to potential doubts about its authority.
This list is part of a larger epistemological framework; not all characteristics are equally emphasized in classical works.
5
Apply the classification
Test yourself by recalling the verse for a given characteristic, or vice versa. This memorization aids in theological argumentation and Qur'anic studies.

First Chapter: First Section - The Development of Epistemology. The civilizational effectiveness: {So do not obey the disbelievers and strive against them with it a great striving} [Al-Furqan 25:52]. 57. Preservation: {And the devils have not come down with it. And it is not appropriate for them, nor are they able. Indeed they are from hearing removed} [Ash-Shu'ara 26:210-212]. And {Indeed, We have sent down the Remembrance, and indeed, We will be its guardians} [Al-Hijr 15:9]. That means its immortality in time and place. Universality: {But it is not except a reminder to the worlds} [Sad 38:87]. Ease: {And We have certainly made the Quran easy for remembrance, so is there any who will remember?} [Al-Qamar 54:17,22,32,40]. Clarity: {And We have certainly sent down to you clear verses} [Al-Baqarah 2:99] and {There has come to you from Allah a light and a clear Book} [Al-Ma'idah 5:15]. 10. Knowledge openness: The Quran is epistemologically open to the beliefs and knowledge of others, as some researchers have realized through verses that call for following the best of what others have, but at the same time it does not forbid knowing their different knowledge (1).
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage enumerates ten characteristics of the Quran related to knowledge, though only six are explicitly listed in the visible text. It begins with 'al-fa'iliyyah al-hadariyyah' (civilizational effectiveness) supported by verse 25:52. Then 'al-hifdh' (preservation) using verses 26:210-212 and 15:9, explaining that preservation implies immortality. Next is 'al-alamiyyah' (universality) with verse 38:87. Then 'al-yusr' (ease) with verse 54:17 and parallels. Then 'al-bayan' (clarity) with verses 2:99 and 5:15. Finally, the tenth characteristic 'al-infitah al-ma'rifi' (knowledge openness) is described: the Quran is open to others' beliefs and knowledge, not prohibiting it, but rather encouraging following the best. The number '57' appears as a likely page number or reference. Each characteristic is accompanied by a Qur'anic proof text.

The Two Directions on Scientific Interpretation
Introduces debate on Quranic knowledge and two views on scientific interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main positions in the debate on scientific interpretation of the Quran and the key scholars associated with each.

Core Idea
The Quran's scope: guidance alone or a source of all knowledge?

This passage begins by affirming the importance of knowledge in the Quran, then immediately introduces a deep disagreement: is the Quran a comprehensive epistemological source, or is it purely a book of guidance? The footnote summarizes the two opposing camps. This lesson helps you map the terrain of this debate before examining the arguments in detail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
In the first paragraph, the author states that scholars disagree about the nature of knowledge in the Quran: does it encompass all types of knowledge, or is it only a book of guidance with passing references? This is the central issue.
The passage uses 'علم' and 'معرفة' interchangeably here.
2
Locate the two directions in the footnote
The long footnote (1) explicitly divides scholars into two directions: al-ittijah al-awwal (rejection) and al-ittijah al-thani (support). Skim the footnote to see the names and brief positions.
The footnote is part of the source text, not a modern addition.
3
Associate key figures with each direction
For the first direction, note Imam al-Shatibi, Sayyid Qutb, Taha Jabir al-Alwani, Umar Ubayd Hasna, Mahmud Ayid al-Rushdan, Muhammad Reda Muharram. For the second, note Muhammad al-Tahir ibn Ashur and Abdul-Majid al-Zindani.
Some figures, like al-Alwani, have a more moderate rejection.
4
Summarize the positions in your own words
Write one sentence for each direction capturing its essence. For the first: 'The Quran should not be interpreted through modern scientific theories because it deviates from Arabic style and subjects revelation to change.' For the second: 'The Quran contains scientific signs that confirm its divine origin, and this is a legitimate area of study.'
This step builds your ability to condense complex positions.

أهمية المعرفة
احتلت المعرفة أهمية بارزة في القرآن الكريم، عندما نعد حديث القرآن عن العلم حديثاً عن المعرفة بصرف النظر عن الجدل الدائر حول المصطلحين. وعلى الرغم من أن خلافاً واسعاً يدور بين العلماء حول طبيعة المعرفة في
القرآن، من حيث شموليتها لكل أنواع المعارف، أو أن القرآن كتاب هداية لا يخرج عن ذلك سوى إشارات عابرة لا يبنى عليها اتجاه، بوصف الهدف الأساسي للقرآن الكريم يكمن في إخراج الناس من الظلمات إلى النور، ومن الضلالة إلى الهداية، دون الدخول في تفصيلات علمية وكونية أخرى(۱). غير
(1) أشير بهذا إلى ما عرف أخيراً باتجاه الإعجاز العلمي في القرآن الكريم، والعلماء على خلاف في هذا ويمكن حصر موقفهم في اتجاهين رئيسين على النحو التالي: الاتجاه الأول: ويتبنى موقف الرفض وإن تفاوتت حدته، ولعل أول قائل بهذا الموقف هو الإمام الشاطبي (ت: ٧٩٠هـ إذ يرى أن تفسير القرآن وفق هذا المنحى يخرجه عن لسان العرب الذي أنزل القرآن بلغتهم، فلا يجوز العدول عن أسلوبهم المعهود في البيان إلى أساليب علمية مستحدثة (الشاطبي، الموافقات في أصول الأحكام، المجلد الأول، ٥٢/٢ - ٦٥ ) فيما يذهب سيد قطب و تابعه بعض الباحثين المعاصرين إلى خشيتهم من إقحام القرآن في قضايا النظريات العلمية المتغيرة، إذ إن ذلك سيسيء إلى القرآن كثيراً إذا ما تبين خلاف ذلك (سيد قطب، في ظلال القرآن، ۱۸۲/۱ - ١٨٤ وانظر مباحث في علوم القرآن لمناع القطان، ۲۷۰ ، والعلوم الطبيعية في ضوء المنهاج الإسلامي وتوجيهاته لأمين كشميري وعثمان عبد الوهاب، ١٤ ). ويحاول البعض أن يعتدل داخل هذا الاتجاه في تقويمه لهذا الموقف فيعتقد أن هذا المنهج مع ما قد يكون له من فوائد إلا أنه لا يشكل منهجاً واضحاً بل لا يعدو عملية انتقاء تهدف إلى التوفيق والمقاربة، عن طريق القياس بين القرآن الكريم والقضايا العلمية، حينما ينطلق من العلم باتجاه القرآن، وليس من القرآن باتجاه العلم. وهذا هو رأي طه جابر العلواني: الجمع بين القراءتين (محاضرة) ١٦٣ هامش (۵). ويذهب بعضهم إلى أبعد من ذلك حين يعدّ كلّ ما يقال في هذا خارجاً عن معنى الإعجاز، ولا يتعدى دلائل النبوة. انظر) هذا الرأي لعمر عبيد حسنة في المدارسة التي أجراها مع الشيخ محمد الغزالي وخرجت بعنوان: كيف نتعامل مع القرآن ١٣٧ - ١٤٢). وقريباً من هذا الرأي ما خلص إليه محمود عايد الرشدان من أن هذا الفهم للقرآن إنما يقيم الحجة على عجز المسلمين أكثر مما يقيمها على إعجاز القرآن النظام المعرفي في القرآن ١٤٠) ويدخل في هذا الاتجاه أيضاً رأي محمد رضا محرم الذي يعدّ فكرة التفسير العلمي بدعة معاصرة (محمد رضا محرم، بدعة تفسير القرآن بالعلم، ١٢٥-١٣٤).
الاتجاه الثاني: وهو على نقيض الأول، وإن تباينت وجهات النظر ،داخله لكن يضمها موقف التأييد إجمالاً. ولعل أول من يطالعنا رده هنا هو محمد الطاهر بن عاشور الذي فقد تخرج الشاطبي المشار إليه آنفا، من سنة وحوه. (ابن عاشور: تفسير التحرير التنوير ٤٤/١ - ٤٥، ۱۲۸ ). أما في الآونة الراهنة فلعل هذا الاتجاه هو الأكثر حضوراً بعد أن تمكن أنصاره من تأسيس هيئة الإعجاز العلمي في القرآن والسنة برابطة العالم الإسلامي. وقد ترأسها ردحاً من الزمن الشيخ / عبدالمجيد الزنداني، والذي يعد من أبرز رواد هذا الاتجاه والمشتغلين بقضاياه، وقد تولى مع بعض رفاقه مناقشة موضوع الإعجاز العلمي في القرآن والسنة والرد على الاتجاه الأول (الزنداني ورفاقه، تأصيل
·
The Importance of Knowledge. Knowledge occupied a prominent importance in the Noble Quran, when we consider the Quran's speech about knowledge as speech about epistemology regardless of the debate around the two terms. Despite a wide disagreement among scholars about the nature of knowledge in the Quran, regarding its comprehensiveness for all types of knowledge, or that the Quran is a book of guidance that does not go beyond that except for passing references on which no direction is built, describing the primary goal of the Noble Quran as taking people out of darkness into light, and from misguidance to guidance, without entering into other scientific and cosmic details(1). However. (1) I refer by this to what has recently become known as the direction of scientific inimitability in the Noble Quran, and scholars differ on this and their position can be summarized into two main directions as follows: The first direction: It adopts the position of rejection, albeit varying in intensity, and perhaps the first proponent of this position is Imam al-Shatibi (d. 790 H) who holds that interpretation of the Quran according to this approach takes it out of the language of the Arabs in which the Quran was revealed, so it is not permissible to deviate from their customary style of expression to new scientific methods (al-Shatibi, al-Muwafaqat fi Usul al-Ahkam, vol. 1, pp. 52/2-65) while Sayyid Qutb and some contemporary researchers following him fear from dragging the Quran into issues of changing scientific theories, as that would harm the Quran greatly if the opposite becomes evident (Sayyid Qutb, Fi Zilal al-Quran, 1/182-184; and see Mabahith fi Ulum al-Quran by Mana' al-Qattan, p. 270; and al-Ulum al-Tabi'iyya fi Daw' al-Manhaj al-Islami wa Tawjihatihi by Amin Kashmiri and Uthman Abd al-Wahhab, p. 14). And some try to be moderate within this direction in evaluating this position, believing that this approach, despite its possible benefits, does not constitute a clear methodology but rather a process of selection aiming at reconciliation and approximation, through analogy between the Noble Quran and scientific issues, when it starts from science towards the Quran, not from the Quran towards science. This is the opinion of Taha Jabir al-Alwani: al-Jam' bayn al-Qira'atayn (lecture) p. 163 footnote (5). And some go further when they consider everything said in this as outside the meaning of inimitability, and does not go beyond proofs of prophecy. See this opinion of Umar Ubayd Hasna in the discussion he conducted with Sheikh Muhammad al-Ghazali and came out titled: Kayfa Nata'amal ma'a al-Quran, pp. 137-142. Close to this opinion is what Mahmud Ayid al-Rushdan concluded that this understanding of the Quran only establishes an argument for the inability of Muslims more than it establishes it for the inimitability of the Quran (al-Nizam al-Ma'rifi fi al-Quran, p. 140). And included in this direction also is the opinion of Muhammad Reda Muharram who considers the idea of scientific interpretation a contemporary innovation (Muhammad Reda Muharram, Bid'at Tafsir al-Quran bi al-Ilm, pp. 125-134). The second direction: It is the opposite of the first, although perspectives within it vary, but they are united by the position of support in general. Perhaps the first whose response we see here is Muhammad al-Tahir ibn Ashur, who outperforms the aforementioned al-Shatibi, from his time onwards (Ibn Ashur: Tafsir al-Tahrir wa al-Tanwir, 1/44-45, p. 128). In the present time, perhaps this direction is the most present after its supporters were able to establish the Authority for Scientific Inimitability in the Quran and Sunnah under the Muslim World League. It was headed for a period of time by Sheikh Abdul-Majid al-Zindani, who is considered one of the most prominent pioneers of this direction and those engaged in its issues, and he with some of his companions undertook the discussion of the topic of scientific inimitability in the Quran and Sunnah and refuting the first direction (al-Zindani and his companions, Ta'sil).
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage discusses the importance of knowledge in the Quran and presents the scholarly debate on the scope of knowledge in the Quran, specifically regarding scientific inimitability. It begins by stating that knowledge is prominent in the Quran, and there is disagreement whether the Quran encompasses all types of knowledge or is purely a book of guidance with only passing references. The primary goal is guidance. Then a long footnote (1) details two main positions. The first direction rejects scientific interpretation; key figures include al-Shatibi, Sayyid Qutb, and others who argue that it deviates from the Arabic style or subjects the Quran to changing theories. Some moderates like Taha Jabir al-Alwani see it as mere reconciliation. Others like Umar Ubayd Hasna and Mahmud Ayid al-Rushdan argue it undermines Muslims' abilities. Muhammad Reda Muharram calls it a bid'a. The second direction supports scientific inimitability; early figure Ibn Ashur, and contemporary supporters like al-Zindani who established the Scientific Inimitability Authority. The passage thus presents an overview of the debate without taking sides.

المعرفة
الإعجاز العلمي
الاتجاه الأول
الاتجاه الثاني

Balancing Scientific Miracles: Three Methodological Controls
Author outlines two trends and proposes a balanced methodology with three controls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three methodological controls for a balanced approach to scientific miracles in the Quran and evaluate whether a given interpretation adheres to them.

Core Idea
The Quran’s scientific miracles are a tool, not the goal; the middle path avoids both rejection and obsession.

This passage from a contemporary work on Quranic epistemology critiques two extreme positions on scientific miracles: complete rejection or uncritical acceptance. The author proposes a middle path grounded in three methodological controls. By understanding these controls, you will be able to critically assess scientific interpretations of the Quran and avoid common pitfalls in this contested area.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extreme positions
The passage references two trends: one that limits the Quran to guidance only, dismissing scientific miracles, and another that enthusiastically embraces every new discovery as a miracle. Note the author's characterization of both.
The first trend is implied in the opening lines about the Quran's purpose being guidance, not detailed science.
2
Locate the author's middle path (al-tawassut)
Find where the author states that the scientific miracle should not be stopped, and that it has benefits for both non-Muslims and believers. This is the pivot to the balanced view.
The phrase 'التوسط' appears in the commentary section.
3
Extract the three methodological controls
The author lists three controls: 1) Do not announce a new scientific miracle until the discovery is settled and uncontested. 2) Use scientific miracles as one method among others, not the sole or dominant approach. 3) Muslims should be active pioneers in discovery, not passive collectors of others' findings.
These are numbered in the Arabic text: ١, ٢, ٣.
4
Reflect on the rationale behind each control
For control 1, the author warns against rushing to conclusions that may later be disproven. For control 2, he emphasizes harmony with the 'Islamization of knowledge' methodology. For control 3, he warns against self-deception and passivity.
Consider how each control addresses a potential harm of the extreme positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الأول: الفصل الأول - تطوّر نظرية المعرفة
القرآن، من حيث شموليتها لكل أنواع المعارف، أو أن القرآن كتاب هداية لا يخرج عن ذلك سوى إشارات عابرة لا يبنى عليها اتجاه، بوصف الهدف الأساسي للقرآن الكريم يكمن في إخراج الناس من الظلمات إلى النور، ومن الضلالة إلى الهداية، دون الدخول في تفصيلات علمية وكونية أخرى(۱). غير أن ما يمثل معقد اتفاق لديهم جميعاً أن للعلم أو المعرفة في القرآن مكانة خاصة تحفل بها كثير من آياته مما يعني توافقاً بين معطيات القرآن والعلم على نحو من التوازي لا التضاد. ذلك أن مصدر العطاء واحد هو الله - تعالى- . وعلى سبيل المثال - لا الحصر - فإن العلماء مقرونون بالله تعالى وملائكته في بعض آيات القرآن الكريم كما في قوله سبحانه وشَهِدَ اللهُ أَنَّهُ لا إِلَهَ إِلَّا .( أما الإعجاز العلمي في القرآن والسنة ). ويساند هذا الاتجاه مشترطاً عدم الاعتساف في فهم نصوصه ذات العلاقة بقضايا العلم محمد عبدالله دراز في كتابه (مدخل إلى القرآن، ۱۷۵ - ۱۷۷ الشيخ عبدالوهاب خلاف فلا يرى بأساً من تفسير القرآن علمياً، بل ولا يلتفت إلى ما يقال عن تبدل النظريات العلمية إذ يعد تفسير النص القرآني أحد وجوه الدلالة فقط فإذا ظهر خطأ النظرية ظهر خطأ فهم الآية (انظر: أصول الفقه، ۲۹ - ۳۰). و ممن يشايع هذا الاتجاه أيضاً: الشيخ محمد متولي الشعراوي الذي يعتمده في تفسيره. (انظر: المختار من تفسير القرآن الجزء الأول ) والشيخ محمد الغزالي كيف نتعامل مع القرآن، ١٣٧ - ١٤٢ )، وحمودة محمد سند الإعجاز العلمي للقرآن والسنة ((النظرية والتطبيق))، ۸۷ - ۸۸ ) وعبدالمجيد النجار، في فقه التدين فهماً وتنزيلاً، ١٠٠/١ - ١٠٤ ). تعليق والرأي الأنسب - فيما يبدو - التوسط، بحيث لا ينبغي إيقاف من الإعجاز العلمي للقرآن، لما له من فوائد جمة سواء على مستوى إظهار معجزات القرآن العلمية على غير المسلمين وبالأخص العلماء المعنيين منهم، فيدفعهم ذلك نحو قراءة القرآن والتفاعل معه بما قد يؤول إلى دخولهم الإسلام، أو على الأقل إنصافه واحترام أهله، أو على المستوى الداخلي في دائرة المؤمنين أنفسهم، إذ تتعزر الحقيقة الإيمانية في قلوبهم، على نحو أعمق إيماناً، وأرسخ يقيناً. بيد أن ذلك يجب أن يتقيد بحملة ضوابط منهجية لعل من أبرزها ما يلي: ١ - التروّي وعدم المسارعة إلى الإعلان عن كل جديد مكتشف حتى يصل إلى نتائجه النهائية غير القابلة للاختلاف أو التبدل. ٢ - الاعتماد على اتجاه الإعجاز العلمي لتحقيق أهداف لا تتعارض ومنهج إسلام المعرفة، بحيث إنه لا ينبغي الارتكاز عليه تماماً، كما لو كان هو الاتجاه الوحيد في تفسير القرآن، أو له من الأفضلية ما يقلّل من شأن المناهج الأخرى. ٣- إن القول بهذا لا يبرر للمسلمين قعودهم عن ولوج عالم التقنية والاكتشافات ليكونوا هم السباقين بالفعل إلى الاكتشاف، ومن ثم مطابقته بما ورد في القرآن الكريم، لا أن يهتموا فقط بتلقط أخبار الاكتشافات العلمية، ثم ينقبوا في المصحف الشريف عن دلالات تطابقها، فإن هذا المسلك يعمق حالة العجز عند المسلمين، اكتفاء بحالة الرصد السلبي هذه، وذلك أحد ضـروب خداع الذات في حقيقة الأمر.
Chapter One: Section One - Development of Epistemology. The Quran, regarding its inclusiveness for all types of knowledge, or that the Quran is a book of guidance not going beyond that except for brief references that an orientation is not built upon, describing the primary goal of the Noble Quran as taking people out of darkness into light, and from misguidance to guidance, without entering into detailed scientific and cosmic matters. However, what represents a common agreement among them all is that science or knowledge in the Quran has a special status that many of its verses are concerned with, indicating a harmony between the data of the Quran and science in a manner of parallelism not contradiction. That is because the source of giving is one, God Almighty. For example, not limited to, scholars are mentioned alongside God and His angels in some verses of the Noble Quran, as in His saying: 'And Allah bears witness that there is no god but He...' (regarding scientific miracles in the Quran and Sunnah). This trend is supported, with the condition of not distorting in understanding its texts related to scientific issues, by Muhammad Abdullah Draz in his book 'Introduction to the Quran' 175-177, and Sheikh Abdul-Wahhab Khallaf, who sees no harm in interpreting the Quran scientifically, and he does not pay attention to what is said about changing scientific theories because he considers the interpretation of the Quranic text as only one aspect of indication; if the theory turns out to be wrong, then the understanding of the verse appears wrong. Among those who also support this trend: Sheikh Muhammad Mutawalli al-Sha'rawi, who relied on it in his interpretation, Sheikh Muhammad al-Ghazali, Hamouda Muhammad Sanad, and Abdulmajeed al-Najjar. Commentary: The most appropriate opinion seems to be the middle path, such that the scientific miracle of the Quran should not be stopped, because of its great benefits, both on the level of showing the scientific miracles of the Quran to non-Muslims, especially concerned scientists, prompting them to read the Quran and interact with it, which may lead to their entry into Islam, or at least to fairness towards it and respect for its people, or on the internal level within the circle of believers themselves, as the truth of faith is fortified in their hearts, deeper in faith and firmer in certainty. However, this must be restricted by methodological controls, the most prominent of which are: 1. Deliberation and not rushing to announce every new discovery until it reaches its final results that are not subject to disagreement or change. 2. Relying on the direction of scientific miracles to achieve goals that do not contradict the methodology of Islamization of knowledge, such that it should not be relied upon entirely, as if it were the only method in interpreting the Quran, or that it has preference that diminishes the importance of other methodologies. 3. Saying this does not justify Muslims' inactivity in entering the world of technology and discoveries to be the actual pioneers of discovery, and then matching it with what is in the Noble Quran, not to only pick up news of scientific discoveries and then search in the Noble Mushaf for indications of their correspondence, for this behavior deepens the state of helplessness among Muslims, content with this negative observation, and that is one form of self-deception in reality.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the place of science in the Quran and the valid methodology of interpreting the Quran through scientific discoveries. The author begins by stating the primary purpose of the Quran: guidance from darkness to light, not detailed scientific exposition. However, he then notes a common agreement that knowledge holds a special place in the Quran, indicating harmony between Quranic data and science. He supports this with an example: the Quran mentions scholars alongside God and angels. The author then lists prominent scholars who endorse scientific interpretation of the Quran, with the condition of not distorting texts. These include Muhammad Abdullah Draz, Abdul-Wahhab Khallaf, Muhammad Mutawalli al-Sha'rawi, and others. The author then presents his own view, which is the middle path: he sees great benefits in demonstrating scientific miracles of the Quran, such as attracting non-Muslims and strengthening believers' faith. However, he insists on three methodological controls: first, delay announcing new discoveries until they are settled; second, use scientific miracles as one method but not the only one, in line with the Islamization of knowledge methodology; third, Muslims should not be passive observers but active pioneers in discovery, matching their findings with the Quran rather than simply collecting others' discoveries. He warns that passive observation is a form of self-deception. The passage thus refines the two extreme positions (complete rejection or uncritical acceptance) by advocating a balanced, methodologically sound approach.

نظرية المعرفة
التوسط

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main purpose of the Quran?
A) To provide detailed scientific explanations of the universe.
B) To guide people from darkness to light and from misguidance to guidance.
C) To prove its miraculous nature through scientific discoveries.
D) To establish a separate epistemology for Muslims.

Key Takeaway
A balanced approach to scientific miracles in the Quran requires three controls: settled science, proportionate use, and active Muslim discovery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a recent claim about a scientific miracle in the Quran (e.g., the expanding universe from Surah al-Dhariyat). Evaluate it using the three controls: (1) Is the scientific theory settled? (2) Is the miracle presented as one of several methods of tafsir? (3) Does the claim promote active Muslim scientific pursuit or passive observation?
?
Write a short paragraph arguing for or against the following statement: 'The Quran contains all scientific knowledge; therefore, Muslims should focus on extracting that knowledge rather than conducting new research.' Use the author's third control to support your argument.
