The Warning Against Selective Knowledge in the Qur'an
Author warns against selective treatment of revelation using the concept of 'adhin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the concept of 'adhin and explain why selective acceptance of revelation is epistemically condemned.

Core Idea
Selective belief in revelation is not faith but a distortion condemned in the Qur'an as 'adhin.

This passage from our coursebook warns against a dangerous approach to divine revelation: picking and choosing which parts to accept based on personal inclination. The Qur'an itself condemns this as 'adhin (making the Qur'an into portions). We will explore the verses that define this deviant path and contrast it with the holistic acceptance required of believers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'adhin
Read the Arabic phrase 'العضين' and note its definition: a deviant approach of dividing revelation into isolated parts, accepting some and rejecting others.
This term appears in Surah Al-Hijr 15:90-91.
2
Distinguish the two groups mentioned
The passage mentions both polytheists (المقتسمين) and some Jewish scholars (أهل الكتاب) who fell into 'adhin. They are cited as negative examples.
A group of Jewish scholars also practiced selective belief, as rebuked in Al-Baqarah 2:85.
3
Contrast with holistic reading (القراءة الكلية)
The passage praises believers who believe in all of the Scripture (آمنوا بالكتاب كله). This holistic acceptance is the opposite of 'adhin.
See Al-Imran 3:119 for the praise of holistic reading.
4
Extract the epistemological principle
The principle is that divine revelation must be accepted in its entirety, not selectively. Selective acceptance leads to disgrace in this life and severe punishment in the next.
This principle applies to all revealed books, including the Qur'an and previous scriptures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التحذير من الانتقائية المعرفية:
ونجد هذا التحذير واضحاً في النهي عن مسلك (العضين) ســــواء عـنـــد المقتسمين من المشركين أو عند أهل الكتاب (۱):
كَما أَنزَلْنَا عَلَى الْمُقْتَسِمِينَ الَّذِينَ جَعَلُوا الْقُرْآنَ عِضِينَ ﴾ [الحجر: ٩٠/١٥-٩١].
و (العضون) مسلك منحرف في التعامل مع الوحي، إذ يقوم على أساس مـن الانتقائية المفرطة في تلاوة القرآن وقراءة آياته بحيث يؤخذ أجزاء متفرقة لا جامع بينها، ولا رابط (۲) ، مما ينتج عن ذلك قبول ورفض مبني كذلك على تلك القراءة الانتقائية غير الموضوعية.
لقد توعد الله تعالى بعض علماء اليهود حين سلكوا هذا المسلك بالخزي في الدنيا والعذاب في الآخرة:
﴿أَقْتُؤْمِنُونَ بِبَعْضِ الْكِتابِ وَتَكْفُرُونَ بِبَعْضٍ فَمَا جَزَاءُ مَنْ يَفْعَلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْكُمْ إِلا خِزْيٌ فِي الْحَيَاةِ الدُّنْيا وَيَوْمَ الْقِيامَةِ يُرَدُّونَ إِلَى أَشَدَّ الْعَذَابِ وَمَا اللهُ بِغَافِلٍ عَمَّا تَعْمَلُونَ﴾ [البقرة: ٨٥/٢].
ولكن الله تعالى خاطب المؤمنين ممتدحاً فيهم صفة القراءة الكليـة سـواء للقرآن، أو المعارف الوحي الأخرى: ﴿ها أنتم أولاء تُحِبُّونَهُمْ وَلا يُحِبُّونَكُمْ وَتُؤْمِنُونَ بالْكِتابِ كُلِّهِ وَإِذا لَقُوكُمْ قَالُوا آمَنَّا وَإِذا خَلَوْا عَضُوا عَلَيْكُمُ الْأَنامِلَ مِنَ الْغَيْظِ قُلْ مُوتُوا بِغَيْظِكُمْ إِنَّ اللَّهَ عَلِيمٌ بِذَاتِ الصُّدُورِ﴾ [آل عمران: ۱۱۹/۳]. والمعنى: لايجوز لكم أن توالوهم في حين لا يوالونكم، رغم أنكم تؤمنون بكتب الله جميعها، التي من جملتها كتابهم
Warning against selective knowledge: We find this warning clearly in the prohibition of the path of 'adhin, both among the dividers from the polytheists and among the People of the Book: 'As We sent down upon the dividers who made the Qur'an into portions.' [Al-Hijr 15:90-91]. And 'adhin is a deviant approach to dealing with revelation, based on excessive selectiveness in reciting the Qur'an and reading its verses, such that scattered parts are taken with no commonality or connection, resulting in acceptance and rejection also based on that non-objective selective reading. Allah Almighty threatened some Jewish scholars who followed this path with disgrace in this world and punishment in the Hereafter: 'Then do you believe in part of the Scripture and disbelieve in part? Then what is the recompense for those who do that among you except disgrace in worldly life; and on the Day of Resurrection they will be sent back to the severest of punishment. And Allah is not unaware of what you do.' [Al-Baqarah 2:85]. But Allah Almighty addressed the believers, praising in them the characteristic of holistic reading, whether of the Qur'an or other revealed knowledge: 'Here you are loving them but they are not loving you, while you believe in the Scripture, all of it. And when they meet you, they say, We believe. But when they are alone, they bite their fingertips at you in rage. Say, Die in your rage. Indeed, Allah is Knowing of what is within the breasts.' [Al-Imran 3:119]. The meaning: It is not permissible for you to ally with them when they do not ally with you, despite the fact that you believe in all the Books of Allah, including their Book.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a warning against selective knowledge (الانتقائية المعرفية), focusing on the concept of 'adhin (العضين). The author explains that this warning is clear in the prohibition of the path of 'adhin, which refers to those who divide the Qur'an into portions, treating some as true and others as false. This deviant approach involves excessive selectiveness in reading the Qur'an, taking scattered parts without connection, leading to subjective acceptance and rejection. The author then cites two Qur'anic verses: Al-Baqarah 2:85 rebukes those who believe in part of the Scripture and disbelieve in part, threatening disgrace and severe punishment. In contrast, Al-Imran 3:119 praises believers who believe in all of the Scripture holistically, despite hostility from others. The passage concludes with an interpretation emphasizing the obligation to accept all divine books, not selectively. The passage uses these verses to ground the ethical principle of comprehensive acceptance of revelation.

Key Terms
العضين
الانتقائية المعرفية
القراءة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which term does the passage use to describe the deviant approach of dividing the Qur'an into portions and selectively accepting parts?
A) العضين (al-‘adhin)
B) الكتمان (al-kitman)
C) التلبيس (al-talbis)
D) الانتقائية (al-intiqā’iyya)

Key Takeaway
Believing in only part of divine revelation is a condemned distortion ('adhin); true faith requires holistic acceptance of all revealed knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another Qur'anic verse that condemns selective belief (besides those cited in the passage) and explain how it reinforces the principle of holistic acceptance.
?
Imagine a person who says, 'I believe in the Qur'an but ignore the verses about jihad because they seem harsh.' Identify which concept from this lesson applies and why it is epistemologically flawed.

Linking Knowledge with Action
Author introduces the principle of linking knowledge with action with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the principle of linking knowledge with action using Quranic evidence and identify its role in Islamic education.

Core Idea
Knowledge without action is empty; action without knowledge is blind.

In this lesson, we explore the first ethical principle for knowledge seekers in the Quran: that knowledge must always be accompanied by action. The author presents this as foundational to Islamic education, citing verses that praise those who act on their knowledge and warn against saying what one does not do. This principle separates Islamic epistemology from mere theoretical accumulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Find the term 'اقتران العلم بالعمل' in the Arabic passage and understand its literal meaning: linking knowledge with action.
This term is a key concept in Islamic educational philosophy.
2
Locate the Quranic evidence
Identify the three verses cited: Fussilat 41:33, Al-Ahqaf 46:13, and As-Saff 61:2-3. Note what each verse contributes: praising those who invite and do good, promising security to the upright, and warning against saying what you do not do.
3
Analyze the author's reasoning
Explain how the author uses these verses to conclude that linking knowledge with action is an integral part of Islamic education, and that role models are an effective teaching method.
The author argues that the principle is derived from the Quran itself.
4
Apply to a modern context
Think of an example from Islamic sciences (e.g., Fiqh or Hadith) where a scholar's knowledge must be accompanied by action. How does this principle ensure integrity in scholarship?

الباب الثاني: الفصل الأول أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها

وفي هذا تربية للمتعلّم على توسيع المدارك، وتنمية النظرة الكلية لدى المتعلم والباحث معاً، لتجنب الوقوع في النظرات التجزيئية والتبعيضية الضيّقة. ولن يتحقق ذلك مالم يسهم المنهج بجميع عناصره وأطرافه في تقديم جملة من الخبرات والمعارف الهادفة إلى تحقيق هذه القيمة.

- اقتران العلم بالعمل:
وهذا ما يجسده مثل قول الحق تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ أَحْسَنُ قَوْلاً مِمَّنْ دَعَا إِلَى اللهِ وَعَمِلَ صَالِحاً وَقَالَ إِنَّنِي مِنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ﴾ [فصلت: ٣٣/٤١]. ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ قالُوا رَبُّنَا اللهُ ثُمَّ اسْتَقَامُوا فَلا خَوْفٌ عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا هُمْ يَحْزَنُونَ﴾ [الأحقاف: ١٣/٤٦]. وحذر المؤمنين من خطورة التهاون في هذا المسلك: ﴿يا أَيُّها الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لِمَ تَقُولُونَ ما لا تَفْعَلُونَ، كَبُرَ مَقْتًا عِنْدَ اللهِ أَن تَقُولُوا ما لا تَفْعَلُونَ﴾ [الصف: ٢/٦١-٣]. ومن هذا نستخلص مبدأ اقتران العلم بالعمل بوصفه جزءا لا يتجزأ من فلسفة التربية الإسلامية. كما أن أسلوب القدوة يمثل أسلوبًا هامًا ومؤثراً من أساليب التربية الإسلامية، يعكس واقعية نقل الأفكار النظرية إلى مجال التطبيق، الذي يمكن محاكاته من قبل المتعلمين.

١- التواضع في البحث:
وهذا ما تشير إليه الآية الكريمة في قصة ابني آدم حيث تعلم أحدهما من الحيوان (الغُراب) كيفية مواراة أخيه بعد أن أضناه البحث. وفي ذلك الإشارة خفيّة إلى أهمية التواضع في البحث والإفادة مما عند الآخرين أيا كانت مستوياتهم الخلقية أو المعرفية: ﴿فَبَعَتَ اللهُ غُرَابًا يَبْحَثُ فِي الْأَرْضِ لِيُرِيَهُ كَيْفَ يُوارِي سَوْأَةَ أَخِيهِ قَالَ يا وَيْلَتَى أَعَجَزْتُ أَنْ أَكُونَ مِثْلَ هَذَا الْغُرَابِ فَأُوارِيَ سَوْأَةَ أَخِي فَأَصْبَحَ مِنَ النادِمِينَ﴾ [المائدة: ٣١/٥]. وهنا يجب أن يستشعر المعلّم أنه لا يمتلك كل جوانب المعرفة وتفصيلاتها، فقد يوجد عند المتعلّم ما لا يوجد عنده، ولذا كان التواضع أدبا من آداب المعرفة يستوي أمامه الجميع. وصدق الله: ﴿وَما أُوتِيتُمْ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ إِلَّا قَلِيلاً﴾ [الإسراء: ٨٥/١٧]. وَفَوْقَ كُلِّ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ [يوسف: ٧٦/١٢].

۱۱- تجنب الغرور والعِندية:
وقد قص القرآن عن أقوام بعض الرسل هذه الصفة الذميمة التي يجب ألا يقع فيها طالب المعرفة: ﴿فَلَمّا جَاءَتْهُمْ رُسُلُهُمْ بِالْبَيِّنَاتِ فَرِحُوا بِمَا عِنْدَهُمْ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ وَحَاقَ بِهِمْ ما كانُوا بِهِ يَسْتَهْزِئُونَ﴾ [غافر: ٨٣/٤٠]. وقال على لسان قارون: ﴿قال إنما أُوتِيتُهُ عَلَى عِلْمٍ عِنْدِي أَوَلَمْ يَعْلَمْ أَنَّ اللَّهَ قَدْ أَهْلَكَ مِنْ قَبْلِهِ مِنَ الْقُرُون مَنْ هُوَ أَشَدُّ مِنْهُ قُوَّةً وَأَكْثَرُ جَمْعاً وَلا يُسْأَلُ عَنْ ذُنُوبِهِمُ الْمُجْرِمُونَ﴾ [القصص: ۷۸/۲۸]. إن ذلك أن التربية الإسلامية موصولة بالله تعالى، فهي ربانية يعني المصدر، كما هي ربانية الوجهة والمسلك.
Chapter Two: The First Section: Ethics of Knowledge and Its Unity

And in this is training for the learner to broaden perspectives and develop the holistic view for both the learner and the researcher, to avoid falling into narrow fragmentary and divisive views. And that will not be achieved unless the curriculum, with all its elements and components, contributes to presenting a set of experiences and knowledge aimed at achieving this value.

- Linking knowledge with action:
And this is embodied by the statement of the Truth, Exalted: 'And who is better in speech than one who invites to Allah and does righteousness and says, 'Indeed, I am of the Muslims.'' [Fussilat 41:33]. 'Indeed, those who have said, 'Our Lord is Allah,' and then remained upright - there will be no fear concerning them, nor will they grieve.' [Al-Ahqaf 46:13]. And He warned the believers of the danger of negligence in this path: 'O you who have believed, why do you say what you do not do? Greatly hateful in the sight of Allah is that you say what you do not do.' [As-Saff 61:2-3]. From this we deduce the principle of linking knowledge with action as an integral part of the philosophy of Islamic education. Also, the method of role model represents an important and influential method among the methods of Islamic education, reflecting the realism of transferring theoretical ideas to the field of application, which can be emulated by learners.

1. Humility in research:
And this is indicated by the noble verse in the story of the two sons of Adam, where one of them learned from the animal (the crow) how to bury his brother after research had exhausted him. And in this is a subtle hint to the importance of humility in research and benefiting from what others have, whatever their moral or intellectual levels: 'Then Allah sent a crow searching in the ground to show him how to conceal the disgrace of his brother. He said, 'O woe to me! Have I failed to be like this crow and conceal the disgrace of my brother?' And he became of the regretful.' [Al-Ma'ida 5:31]. Here the teacher must realize that he does not possess all aspects of knowledge and its details, for the learner may have what he does not have. Therefore, humility is an etiquette of knowledge before which everyone is equal. And Allah spoke the truth: 'And you have not been given of knowledge except a little.' [Al-Isra' 17:85]. 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing.' [Yusuf 12:76].

11. Avoiding arrogance and stubbornness:
And the Quran has related about the peoples of some messengers this blameworthy trait which the seeker of knowledge must not fall into: 'And when their messengers came to them with clear proofs, they rejoiced in what they had of knowledge, and there enveloped them that which they used to ridicule.' [Ghafir 40:83]. And He said on the tongue of Qarun: 'He said, 'I was only given it because of knowledge I have.' Did he not know that Allah had destroyed before him among the generations those who were stronger than him in power and greater in accumulation? But the criminals will not be asked about their sins.' [Al-Qasas 28:78]. That is because Islamic education is connected to Allah Almighty; it is divine in source, and it is divine in orientation and path.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage, from a contemporary work on Quranic epistemology, enumerates three ethical principles that must govern the pursuit of knowledge in Islam. The author begins by emphasizing the training of the learner to develop a holistic view, avoiding fragmentary approaches. Then he lists the first principle: linking knowledge with action. He supports this with Quranic verses that commend those who invite to Allah and do righteous deeds (Fussilat 41:33), promise security to those who remain upright (Al-Ahqaf 46:13), and warn against saying what one does not do (As-Saff 61:2-3). He concludes that this principle is integral to Islamic educational philosophy and that role models are an effective method for translating theory into practice. The second principle is humility in research. The author draws from the story of Cain and Abel, where Cain learned from a crow how to bury his brother (Al-Ma'ida 5:31). This illustrates that a seeker of knowledge must be humble enough to learn from any source, regardless of its apparent status. The teacher must recognize that learners may possess knowledge he lacks. He cites verses stating that humanity has been given only little knowledge (Al-Isra' 17:85) and that above every knowledgeable person is the All-Knowing (Yusuf 12:76). The third principle is avoiding arrogance and stubbornness. The author cites the Quranic condemnation of those who rejoiced in their own knowledge when messengers came with clear proofs and were ultimately destroyed (Ghafir 40:83), and the example of Qarun who attributed his wealth to his own knowledge and was punished (Al-Qasas 28:78). He concludes that Islamic education is divine in both source and direction.

Key Terms
اقتران العلم بالعمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which verse does the author cite to warn against saying what one does not do?
A) Fussilat 41:33
B) Al-Ahqaf 46:13
C) As-Saff 61:2-3
D) Al-Ma'ida 5:31

Key Takeaway
The principle of linking knowledge with action is essential in Islamic education; true knowledge manifests in practice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the verse ‘Why do you say what you do not do?’ (As-Saff 61:2-3) and write a brief explanation of how this applies to a student of Islamic knowledge.
?
Give an example from the life of a companion (e.g., Abu Bakr or Umar) where knowledge and action were inseparable.

Humility in Research
Author illustrates humility in research using the story of Adam's sons and the crow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic evidence for humility in seeking knowledge and explain its importance using the story of Cain and Abel.

Core Idea
Humility in research means learning from any source, even from a crow.

The second principle is humility in research. The author uses the striking story of one of Adam's sons learning to bury his brother from a crow. This teaches that no source of knowledge is too lowly, and that a true seeker must be humble. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and application.

Guided Path
1
Locate the story
Find the Quranic verse (Al-Ma'ida 5:31) in the Arabic passage. It tells of a crow sent by Allah to show the son of Adam how to bury his brother.
The verse is quoted in full within the passage.
2
Identify the lesson
What does the son learn from the crow? He learns the practical skill of burial. The author interprets this as teaching humility: even from an animal, knowledge can be gained.
3
Analyze the author's inference
How does the author derive the principle of humility? He connects the story to the importance of learning from others regardless of status, and the teacher's recognition that students may possess knowledge the teacher lacks.
The author then cites verses 17:85 and 12:76 to reinforce that human knowledge is limited.
4
Reflect on the teacher-student dynamic
Why must a teacher be humble before learners? Because no one possesses all knowledge. The learner may have insights the teacher does not.
Humility is an etiquette of knowledge (adab al-ma'rifa).

And in this is training for the learner to broaden perspectives and develop the holistic view for both the learner and the researcher, to avoid falling into narrow fragmentary and divisive views. And this is indicated by the noble verse in the story of the two sons of Adam, where one of them learned from the animal (the crow) how to bury his brother after research had exhausted him. 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing.' [Yusuf 12:76].

And the Quran has related about the peoples of some messengers this blameworthy trait which the seeker of knowledge must not fall into: 'And when their messengers came to them with clear proofs, they rejoiced in what they had of knowledge, and there enveloped them that which they used to ridicule.' [Ghafir 40:83]. And He said on the tongue of Qarun: 'He said, 'I was only given it because of knowledge I have.' Did he not know that Allah had destroyed before him among the generations those who were stronger than him in power and greater in accumulation? But the criminals will not be asked about their sins.' [Al-Qasas 28:78]. That is because Islamic education is connected to Allah Almighty; it is divine in source, and it is divine in orientation and path.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage, from a contemporary work on Quranic epistemology, enumerates three ethical principles that must govern the pursuit of knowledge in Islam. The author begins by emphasizing the training of the learner to develop a holistic view, avoiding fragmentary approaches. Then he lists the first principle: linking knowledge with action. He supports this with Quranic verses that commend those who invite to Allah and do righteous deeds (Fussilat 41:33), promise security to those who remain upright (Al-Ahqaf 46:13), and warn against saying what one does not do (As-Saff 61:2-3). He concludes that this principle is integral to Islamic educational philosophy and that role models are an effective method for translating theory into practice. The second principle is humility in research. The author draws from the story of Cain and Abel, where Cain learned from a crow how to bury his brother (Al-Ma'ida 5:31). This illustrates that a seeker of knowledge must be humble enough to learn from any source, regardless of its apparent status. The teacher must recognize that learners may possess knowledge he lacks. He cites verses stating that humanity has been given only little knowledge (Al-Isra' 17:85) and that above every knowledgeable person is the All-Knowing (Yusuf 12:76). The third principle is avoiding arrogance and stubbornness. The author cites the Quranic condemnation of those who rejoiced in their own knowledge when messengers came with clear proofs and were ultimately destroyed (Ghafir 40:83), and the example of Qarun who attributed his wealth to his own knowledge and was punished (Al-Qasas 28:78). He concludes that Islamic education is divine in both source and direction.

Avoiding Arrogance and Stubbornness
Author warns against arrogance and stubbornness with examples from Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic warnings against arrogance in knowledge and relate them to the ethical conduct of a knowledge seeker.

Core Idea
Arrogance in knowledge leads to destruction; humility opens the door to truth.

The third principle warns against arrogance and stubbornness. The author cites the fate of those who rejected messengers because they were proud of their own knowledge, and Qarun who attributed his wealth to his knowledge. This lesson examines these examples and their relevance to Islamic education.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condemned trait
Find the phrase 'الغرور والعِندية' (arrogance and stubbornness) in the Arabic. Understand that these are blameworthy traits for a knowledge seeker.
2
Analyze the first Quranic example
Read Ghafir 40:83: people who rejoiced in their own knowledge when messengers brought clear proofs, and were destroyed. What error did they commit? They were arrogant and ridiculed the truth.
The verse is quoted in full: 'فَلَمّا جَاءَتْهُمْ رُسُلُهُمْ بِالْبَيِّنَاتِ فَرِحُوا بِمَا عِنْدَهُمْ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ...'
3
Analyze the second example: Qarun
Read Qasas 28:78: Qarun said his wealth came from his own knowledge, failing to recognize Allah's power. His arrogance led to his destruction.
The author contrasts Qarun's statement with the fact that Allah destroys those stronger and richer.
4
Draw the lesson for knowledge seekers
Why must a student avoid arrogance? Because it prevents acceptance of truth and leads to destruction. The author concludes that Islamic education is divine in source and direction, so it demands humility.
The concluding sentence ties the principle to the divine nature of Islamic education.

الباب الثاني: الفصل الأول أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها

وفي هذا تربية للمتعلّم على توسيع المدارك، وتنمية النظرة الكلية لدى المتعلم والباحث معاً، لتجنب الوقوع في النظرات التجزيئية والتبعيضية الضيّقة. ولن يتحقق ذلك مالم يسهم المنهج بجميع عناصره وأطرافه في تقديم جملة من الخبرات والمعارف الهادفة إلى تحقيق هذه القيمة.

- اقتران العلم بالعمل:
وهذا ما يجسده مثل قول الحق تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ أَحْسَنُ قَوْلاً مِمَّنْ دَعَا إِلَى اللهِ وَعَمِلَ صَالِحاً وَقَالَ إِنَّنِي مِنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ﴾ [فصلت: ٣٣/٤١]. ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ قالُوا رَبُّنَا اللهُ ثُمَّ اسْتَقَامُوا فَلا خَوْفٌ عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا هُمْ يَحْزَنُونَ﴾ [الأحقاف: ١٣/٤٦]. وحذر المؤمنين من خطورة التهاون في هذا المسلك: ﴿يا أَيُّها الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لِمَ تَقُولُونَ ما لا تَفْعَلُونَ، كَبُرَ مَقْتًا عِنْدَ اللهِ أَن تَقُولُوا ما لا تَفْعَلُونَ﴾ [الصف: ٢/٦١-٣]. ومن هذا نستخلص مبدأ اقتران العلم بالعمل بوصفه جزءا لا يتجزأ من فلسفة التربية الإسلامية. كما أن أسلوب القدوة يمثل أسلوبًا هامًا ومؤثراً من أساليب التربية الإسلامية، يعكس واقعية نقل الأفكار النظرية إلى مجال التطبيق، الذي يمكن محاكاته من قبل المتعلمين.

١- التواضع في البحث:
وهذا ما تشير إليه الآية الكريمة في قصة ابني آدم حيث تعلم أحدهما من الحيوان (الغُراب) كيفية مواراة أخيه بعد أن أضناه البحث. وفي ذلك الإشارة خفيّة إلى أهمية التواضع في البحث والإفادة مما عند الآخرين أيا كانت مستوياتهم الخلقية أو المعرفية: ﴿فَبَعَتَ اللهُ غُرَابًا يَبْحَثُ فِي الْأَرْضِ لِيُرِيَهُ كَيْفَ يُوارِي سَوْأَةَ أَخِيهِ قَالَ يا وَيْلَتَى أَعَجَزْتُ أَنْ أَكُونَ مِثْلَ هَذَا الْغُرَابِ فَأُوارِيَ سَوْأَةَ أَخِي فَأَصْبَحَ مِنَ النادِمِينَ﴾ [المائدة: ٣١/٥]. وهنا يجب أن يستشعر المعلّم أنه لا يمتلك كل جوانب المعرفة وتفصيلاتها، فقد يوجد عند المتعلّم ما لا يوجد عنده، ولذا كان التواضع أدبا من آداب المعرفة يستوي أمامه الجميع. وصدق الله: ﴿وَما أُوتِيتُمْ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ إِلَّا قَلِيلاً﴾ [الإسراء: ٨٥/١٧]. وَفَوْقَ كُلِّ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٌ﴾ [يوسف: ٧٦/١٢].

۱۱- تجنب الغرور والعِندية:
وقد قص القرآن عن أقوام بعض الرسل هذه الصفة الذميمة التي يجب ألا يقع فيها طالب المعرفة: ﴿فَلَمّا جَاءَتْهُمْ رُسُلُهُمْ بِالْبَيِّنَاتِ فَرِحُوا بِمَا عِنْدَهُمْ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ وَحَاقَ بِهِمْ ما كانُوا بِهِ يَسْتَهْزِئُونَ﴾ [غافر: ٨٣/٤٠]. وقال على لسان قارون: ﴿قال إنما أُوتِيتُهُ عَلَى عِلْمٍ عِنْدِي أَوَلَمْ يَعْلَمْ أَنَّ اللَّهَ قَدْ أَهْلَكَ مِنْ قَبْلِهِ مِنَ الْقُرُون مَنْ هُوَ أَشَدُّ مِنْهُ قُوَّةً وَأَكْثَرُ جَمْعاً وَلا يُسْأَلُ عَنْ ذُنُوبِهِمُ الْمُجْرِمُونَ﴾ [القصص: ۷۸/۲۸]. إن ذلك أن التربية الإسلامية موصولة بالله تعالى، فهي ربانية يعني المصدر، كما هي ربانية الوجهة والمسلك.
Chapter Two: The First Section: Ethics of Knowledge and Its Unity

And in this is training for the learner to broaden perspectives and develop the holistic view for both the learner and the researcher, to avoid falling into narrow fragmentary and divisive views. And that will not be achieved unless the curriculum, with all its elements and components, contributes to presenting a set of experiences and knowledge aimed at achieving this value.

- Linking knowledge with action:
And this is embodied by the statement of the Truth, Exalted: 'And who is better in speech than one who invites to Allah and does righteousness and says, 'Indeed, I am of the Muslims.'' [Fussilat 41:33]. 'Indeed, those who have said, 'Our Lord is Allah,' and then remained upright - there will be no fear concerning them, nor will they grieve.' [Al-Ahqaf 46:13]. And He warned the believers of the danger of negligence in this path: 'O you who have believed, why do you say what you do not do? Greatly hateful in the sight of Allah is that you say what you do not do.' [As-Saff 61:2-3]. From this we deduce the principle of linking knowledge with action as an integral part of the philosophy of Islamic education. Also, the method of role model represents an important and influential method among the methods of Islamic education, reflecting the realism of transferring theoretical ideas to the field of application, which can be emulated by learners.

1. Humility in research:
And this is indicated by the noble verse in the story of the two sons of Adam, where one of them learned from the animal (the crow) how to bury his brother after research had exhausted him. And in this is a subtle hint to the importance of humility in research and benefiting from what others have, whatever their moral or intellectual levels: 'Then Allah sent a crow searching in the ground to show him how to conceal the disgrace of his brother. He said, 'O woe to me! Have I failed to be like this crow and conceal the disgrace of my brother?' And he became of the regretful.' [Al-Ma'ida 5:31]. Here the teacher must realize that he does not possess all aspects of knowledge and its details, for the learner may have what he does not have. Therefore, humility is an etiquette of knowledge before which everyone is equal. And Allah spoke the truth: 'And you have not been given of knowledge except a little.' [Al-Isra' 17:85]. 'And above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing.' [Yusuf 12:76].

11. Avoiding arrogance and stubbornness:
And the Quran has related about the peoples of some messengers this blameworthy trait which the seeker of knowledge must not fall into: 'And when their messengers came to them with clear proofs, they rejoiced in what they had of knowledge, and there enveloped them that which they used to ridicule.' [Ghafir 40:83]. And He said on the tongue of Qarun: 'He said, 'I was only given it because of knowledge I have.' Did he not know that Allah had destroyed before him among the generations those who were stronger than him in power and greater in accumulation? But the criminals will not be asked about their sins.' [Al-Qasas 28:78]. That is because Islamic education is connected to Allah Almighty; it is divine in source, and it is divine in orientation and path.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage, from a contemporary work on Quranic epistemology, enumerates three ethical principles that must govern the pursuit of knowledge in Islam. The author begins by emphasizing the training of the learner to develop a holistic view, avoiding fragmentary approaches. Then he lists the first principle: linking knowledge with action. He supports this with Quranic verses that commend those who invite to Allah and do righteous deeds (Fussilat 41:33), promise security to those who remain upright (Al-Ahqaf 46:13), and warn against saying what one does not do (As-Saff 61:2-3). He concludes that this principle is integral to Islamic educational philosophy and that role models are an effective method for translating theory into practice. The second principle is humility in research. The author draws from the story of Cain and Abel, where Cain learned from a crow how to bury his brother (Al-Ma'ida 5:31). This illustrates that a seeker of knowledge must be humble enough to learn from any source, regardless of its apparent status. The teacher must recognize that learners may possess knowledge he lacks. He cites verses stating that humanity has been given only little knowledge (Al-Isra' 17:85) and that above every knowledgeable person is the All-Knowing (Yusuf 12:76). The third principle is avoiding arrogance and stubbornness. The author cites the Quranic condemnation of those who rejoiced in their own knowledge when messengers came with clear proofs and were ultimately destroyed (Ghafir 40:83), and the example of Qarun who attributed his wealth to his own knowledge and was punished (Al-Qasas 28:78). The Quranic Concept of Unity of Knowledge
Author explains the Quranic concept of unity of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic foundation for the unity of knowledge and identify its evidence from revelation.

Core Idea
All knowledge in the Quran is unified by tawhid, a single origin, and a single destiny.

The passage explores how the Quran grounds the unity of knowledge in the oneness of God, the unity of natural laws, and the unity of origin and destiny. This concept is not merely philosophical but is presented as a mark of the truth of revelation, validated by the consensus of scholars and the Quran's own call for a single religion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the multiple unities
Locate the five manifestations of unity the author lists: unity of the worshipped God, unity of laws, unity of the force emanating those laws, unity of nations, and unity of origin and destiny.
These unities collectively define the Quranic worldview.
2
Analyze the unified answer of revelation
Notice how the author argues that the strength of religious truth lies in its unified answer about the source and destiny of the universe: God is the source and return.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the quoted verse (21:92) and see how it supports the call for a single religion, reinforcing the unity of truth.
The verse emphasizes ummatan wahidatan (one nation/norm).
4
Reflect on the Abrahamic axis
Consider how the author positions the Abrahamic Hanifiyya religion as the core around which all revealed knowledge revolves.
This is consistent with the Quran's claim of confirming previous scriptures.

فلسفة وحدة المعرفة

تنبثق وحدة المعرفة في القرآن من الإيمان بحقيقة الوحدة في الكون وحدة الإله المعبود ووحدة القوانين الناظمة لأجزاء الكون، ووحدة القوة الصادرة عنها تلك القوانين ووحدة الأمم التي آمنت بهذا الدين، ثمّ وحدة المنشأ والمصير. وفي ضوء مظاهر الوحدة تلك يتحدد معنى وحدانية الله والصلة بين الإنسان وربه، والكون وموجوداته(۱).
ولعلّ من أول ملامح القوة في الحقيقة الدينية على الإطلاق ذلك الأسلوب الذي قدّمت به هذه الحقيقة في معارف الوحي، حيث اتحدت إجابتها بدقة في تحديد مصدر الكون ومصيره. فالله هو مصدر الكون، ومصيره إليه. ومما يدل على صدق هذه الإجابة أن اتفاق أهل العلم والاختصاص من بقاع شتى، وفي حقب زمنية مختلفة، على هذه الإجابة، يجعل منها علامة صحة على هذه الفكرة. فوحدة المعرفة تعني وحدة الحقيقة التي دعا إليها القرآن الكريم في غير
ما آية: ﴿ إِنَّ هَذِهِ أُمَّتُكُمْ أُمَّةً واحِدَةً وَأَنا رَبُّكُمْ فَاعْبُدُونِ﴾ [الأنبياء: ۱۲/۲۱].
وما جاءت بذرة الخلاف والفرقة في الرسالات السماوية السابقة إلا بعد ذهاب المرسلين، ومجيء أجيال لاحقة اختلفت فمنهم من آمن، ومنهم من کفر (۲)
وفي مواضع من القرآن الكريم تتجسّد معاني الوحدة المعرفية بين معارف الوحي جميعها بصيغ مختلفة. فالقرآن يركز ابتداء على أن الملة الإبراهيمية الحنيفية هي مستند القرآن في دعوته التوحيدية، مما يضفي على الإسلام كله هذه الصفة، ويجعل من هذه الملة محورًاً جوهريا تدور حوله جميع معارف الوحي:
Philosophy of the Unity of Knowledge

The unity of knowledge in the Quran arises from belief in the reality of unity in the universe: the unity of the worshipped God, the unity of the laws governing the parts of the universe, the unity of the force emanating those laws, the unity of the nations that believed in this religion, and then the unity of origin and destiny. In light of these manifestations of unity, the meaning of the oneness of Allah, the relationship between man and his Lord, and the universe and its existents is determined(1).
Perhaps among the first signs of strength in the religious truth ever is that method by which this truth was presented in the knowledge of revelation, where its answer was precisely unified in determining the source of the universe and its destiny. So Allah is the source of the universe, and its destiny is to Him. And among what indicates the truth of this answer is that the agreement of people of knowledge and specialization from various regions, and in different time periods, on this answer makes it a sign of the correctness of this idea. Thus, the unity of knowledge means the unity of truth which the Holy Quran called for in many verses: 'Indeed this, your religion, is one religion, and I am your Lord, so worship Me' [Al-Anbiya 21:92].
And the seed of disagreement and division in previous heavenly messages only came after the departure of the messengers, and the coming of later generations that differed, some believing, some disbelieving (2).
In places of the Holy Quran, the meanings of epistemological unity among all revelation knowledge are embodied in different forms. The Quran emphasizes from the beginning that the Abrahamic Hanifiyya religion is the basis of the Quran in its monotheistic call, which gives all Islam this characteristic, and makes this religion a fundamental axis around which all revelation knowledge revolves.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage expounds the Quranic philosophy of the unity of knowledge (waḥdat al-maʿrifa). It states that this unity is rooted in belief in the oneness of God, the unity of natural laws, the unity of the force behind those laws, the unity of believing nations, and the unity of origin and destiny. These manifold unities define the meaning of tawḥīd and the relationship between humanity, God, and the cosmos. The author argues that the strength of religious truth is evident in the way revelation gives a unified answer regarding the source and destiny of the universe: God is the source and return. This answer is validated by the agreement of knowledgeable people across time and place. The Quran's call for a single religion (ummatan wāḥidatan) underscores the unity of truth. The passage further notes that divisions in previous religions occurred only after the prophets were gone. Finally, the Abrahamic monotheistic tradition (al-milla al-ibrāhīmiyya al-ḥanīfiyya) is presented as the core around which all revealed knowledge revolves.

Key Terms
وحدة المعرفة
وحدة الإله
وحدة القوانين
وحدة المنشأ والمصير
الملة الإبراهيمية الحنيفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what evidence supports the truth of the answer that God is the source and destiny of the universe?
A) The Quran's linguistic miracles
B) The agreement of scholars and specialists across time and place
C) The historical spread of Islam
D) The logical necessity of a first cause

Key Takeaway
The Quran establishes a unified worldview where all knowledge originates from the one God, returns to Him, and is validated by scholarly consensus across time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Discuss with a partner: How does the agreement of scholars across time and place serve as evidence for the truth of revelation? Is this logical argument valid in Islamic epistemology? Provide reasons.

The Quranic Foundation of Knowledge Unity in Abrahamic Monotheism
Author argues Quranic unity of knowledge from Abrahamic monotheism and derives educational principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the Quran grounds the unity of knowledge in Abrahamic hanifiyya and explain why this eliminates division among prophetic traditions.

Core Idea
All prophetic calls share a single source; the Quran unites them by returning to Abraham's original monotheism.

This lesson explores the first part of the passage, where the author establishes the Quranic basis for the unity of knowledge. The Quran does not create a new religion but extends the Abrahamic monotheistic creed. By grounding all knowledge in tawhid, the Quran elevates people above schism and provides a logical reason for unity across all revelations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic claim about Abraham's religion
Find the verses cited (Al Imran 3:67-68 and An-Nahl 16:123) and note how the author describes Abraham as neither Jew nor Christian but a hanif Muslim. This establishes a pre-Islamic monotheistic root.
The term 'hanif' denotes a pure monotheist.
2
Trace the author's argument for continuity
The author argues that Islam, its Messenger, and its followers are merely an extension of Abraham's path. This creates continuity and unity among all prophetic messages.
3
Explain why this implies unity of knowledge
If all prophets came from the same divine source, then all revealed knowledge is unified. The Quran's method of calling others appeals to this original unity, which noble souls embrace.
4
Derive the consequence: Muslims extend Abraham's path
The logical conclusion is that Muslims carry forward the same monotheistic mission. There is no fundamental conflict between Islam and earlier revelations when understood from this perspective.

الباب الثاني: الفصل الأول - أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها. القرآن أن المسلم يشترك مع جميع دعوات السماء في الإيمان بمبادئهم جميعا. وعندئذ يعلو الناس فوق الانشقاق والتنافس فلا يبقى مبرر منطقي يدفع لشيء من ذلك. وهذا أسلوب القرآن في دعوته الآخرين حتى تنفتح له قلوبهم المتلهفة، لأن الوحدة الأصلية يستجيب لها، ويعتز بها ذوو النفوس السامية جميع. ولهذا فإن الإسلام ورسوله وأتباعه إنما يمثلون امتدادا للملة الإبراهيمية الحنيفية ليس أكثر. وما كان إبْراهِيمُ يَهُودِيًا وَلا نَصْرانِيّاً وَلَكِنْ كانَ حَنِيفاً مُسْلِماً وَما كان مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ، إِنَّ أَوْلَى النَّاسِ بِإِبْرَاهِيمَ لَلَّذِينَ اتَّبَعُوهُ وَهَذَا النَّبِيُّ وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَاللَّهُ وَلِيُّ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ﴾ [آل عمران: ٦٧/٣-٦٨]. وأُمر الرسول بلزوم الملة الحنيفية الإبراهيمية صراحة: ثُمَّ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَيْكَ أَنِ اتَّبِعْ مِلَّةَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ حَنِيفاً وَمَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ [النحل: ١٢٣/١٦]. ويمكن استخلاص المضامين التربوية التالية في ضوء ما تقدّم: ۱ - إنّ وحدة المعرفة تتضمن في أساسها التربوي فلسفة الوحدانية، التي هي ركيزة أساسية من ركائز التربية الإسلامية بوجه عام، ونظرية المعرفة في القرآن بوجه خاص. ٢ - بناء منهج تكاملي انبثاقا من فكرة وحدة المعرفة. -- يهدف المنهج إلى إخراج المتعلّم الموحد، وعليه فإن مفردات المحتوى يجب أن تشمل قيمة التوحيد بصورته الفاعلة الجامعة لطوائف الموحدين من أتباع معارف الوحي. ٤- لقد أصبح من المألوف القول بأن علمًا ما لا ينبغي اختزاله إلى علم آخر، وإن الظواهر الاجتماعية ينبغي تفسيرها بواسطة العلوم الاجتماعية لا بعلوم أخرى. ومن جهة أخرى يبدو من المستحيل في التسلسل الهرمي للعلوم وخاصة اليوم، عزل أي فرع من فروع العلم عن النظام الكلي للمعرفة العلمية. فاستقلال أيّ علم هو استقلال نسبي. وتبدو الصلة بين العلوم مطلقة، ولو أن طبيعة وتركيب هذه الصلة تتغير من حال لأخرى. إن تكامل العلوم على هذا النحو - بما في ذلك العلوم الطبيعية - دفع بالأستاذ إسماعيل الفاروقي لأن يطلق على العلوم الاجتماعية (علوم الأمة) لأنها متعلقة بخلافة الإنسان. وإذا كانت هذه الخلافة اجتماعية في جوهرها فإن هذه العلوم تصبح (علوم أمة لأنها غير متعارضة مع العلوم الطبيعية. ويؤكد هذا وحدة الهدف التربوي، فإذا كان هدف التعليم الإسلامي هي تنشئة الإنسان العابد الصالح - بحسب توصيات المؤتمر العالمي الأول للتعليم الإسلامي - فإن علوم الشريعة تلتقي مع بقيّة العلوم من طب وهندسة ورياضيات وعلم نفس واجتماع... إلخ إذ الكل علوم إسلامية ما دامت في الإطار الإسلامي التزاما وانسجاما مع المفهوم والتصور الخاص بـه، وذلك لارتباط المجالين كليهما بتحقيق هدف سيادة الإنسان على الأشياء والأحياء في الأرض، أي هدف الاستخلاف باعتباره هدفًا وجوديًا أسمى وغائيا. وإذا كان المتفق عليه حديثًا أن العلم مادة بناء المعرفة، وطريقة التفكير من
Chapter Two: First Section - Ethics of Knowledge and its Unity. The Quran that the Muslim shares with all heavenly calls in belief in their principles all. And then people rise above schism and competition, so no logical justification remains to drive any of that. And this is the Quran's method in calling others so that their eager hearts open to it, because the original unity is responded to by it, and those with noble souls all take pride in it. And for this, Islam, its Messenger, and its followers only represent an extension of the Abrahamic monotheistic creed, nothing more. And Abraham was neither a Jew nor a Christian but was a monotheist Muslim, and he was not of the polytheists. Indeed, the nearest of people to Abraham are those who followed him and this Prophet and those who believed, and Allah is the protector of the believers. [Al Imran 3:67-68]. And the Messenger was commanded to adhere to the Abrahamic monotheistic creed explicitly: Then We revealed to you to follow the creed of Abraham, a monotheist, and he was not of the polytheists. [An-Nahl 16:123]. And the following educational implications can be derived in light of what preceded: 1 - That the unity of knowledge includes in its educational foundation the philosophy of monotheism, which is a fundamental pillar of Islamic education in general, and the theory of knowledge in the Quran in particular. 2 - Building an integrative curriculum emanating from the idea of unity of knowledge. The curriculum aims to produce the monotheist learner, and therefore the content items must include the value of monotheism in its active form uniting the groups of monotheists from followers of the knowledge of revelation. 4 - It has become common to say that a science should not be reduced to another science, and that social phenomena should be explained by social sciences, not by other sciences. On the other hand, it seems impossible in the hierarchy of sciences, especially today, to isolate any branch of science from the total system of scientific knowledge. So the independence of any science is relative. And the connection between sciences appears absolute, even though the nature and structure of this connection changes from one case to another. This integration of sciences - including natural sciences - prompted Professor Ismail al-Faruqi to call social sciences 'sciences of the ummah' because they pertain to human khilafah (vicegerency). And if this khilafah is social in essence, then these sciences become 'sciences of the ummah' because they are not in conflict with natural sciences. This affirms the unity of the educational goal. If the goal of Islamic education is nurturing the righteous worshipper - according to the recommendations of the first World Conference on Islamic Education - then sharia sciences meet with other sciences like medicine, engineering, mathematics, psychology, sociology, etc., for all are Islamic sciences as long as they remain within the Islamic framework, commitment and harmony with its particular concept and perception, due to the connection of both fields to achieving the goal of human sovereignty over things and living beings on earth, i.e., the goal of vicegerency as a supreme existential and teleological goal. And if it is agreed upon recently that science is the material of knowledge building and the method of thinking from
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition on the unity of knowledge in the Quran and its educational implications. The author begins by stating that the Quran requires the Muslim to share in the principles of all heavenly (divinely revealed) calls, thereby elevating people above division and competition. This is presented as the Quran's method of inviting others, appealing to their eager hearts because they respond to original unity (fitrah). The author then grounds this unity in the Abrahamic tradition, citing two Quranic verses: one stating Abraham was neither Jew nor Christian but a monotheist (hanif) Muslim, and another commanding the Prophet to follow Abraham's creed. The conclusion is that Islam and its followers are merely an extension of Abraham's monotheistic path. From this, the author derives educational implications in a numbered list. The first implication is that the unity of knowledge encompasses the philosophy of monotheism (tawhid), which is a core pillar of Islamic education and the Quranic theory of knowledge. The second calls for building an integrative curriculum emanating from the idea of unity of knowledge, aimed at producing a monotheist learner; thus, curriculum content must include tawhid as an active, unifying value for all monotheists following revealed knowledge. The fourth point (point 3 is absent) discusses the relative independence of sciences: no science should be reduced to another, and social phenomena require social sciences, yet in the hierarchy of sciences, no branch can be isolated from the total system of scientific knowledge. This integration led Isma'il al-Faruqi to term social sciences 'sciences of the ummah' because they relate to human vicegerency (khilafah). If vicegerency is social, these sciences are not opposed to natural sciences. The author concludes that this affirms the unity of the educational goal: if Islamic education aims to nurture the righteous worshipper, then all sciences (sharia, medicine, engineering, etc.) are Islamic if they remain within the Islamic framework and contribute to human sovereignty over creation, i.e., the goal of vicegerency as a supreme existential objective. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence about science as material of knowledge and method of thinking.

Key Terms
وحدة المعرفة (wahdat al-ma'rifa)
الملة الحنيفية (al-millat al-hanifiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Quranic basis for unity of knowledge?
A) The Quran rejects all forms of knowledge except revelation.
B) The Quran builds on the Abrahamic monotheistic tradition to unite all prophetic calls.
C) The Quran permits only social sciences.
D) The Quran separates religious and worldly knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Quran unifies knowledge by returning to Abraham's original monotheistic creed, making all prophetic calls extensions of one divine source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Find another Quranic verse that emphasizes the unity of prophetic messages (e.g., Al-Baqarah 2:136) and explain how it reinforces the author's argument that the Muslim shares in all heavenly calls.
?
2. Explain in one paragraph why, according to this passage, the claim that 'Islam is a completely new religion' contradicts the Quranic logic of continuity.

Educational Implications: Tawhid as the Core of the Curriculum
Author argues Quranic unity of knowledge from Abrahamic monotheism and derives educational principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive from the unity of knowledge the principle that Islamic education must center on tawhid and aim for vicegerency.

Core Idea
Unity of knowledge demands a curriculum where every discipline serves tawhid and prepares for khilafah.

In this lesson, we examine the numbered educational implications the author extracts from the Quranic unity of knowledge. The first implication establishes that the philosophy of monotheism (tawhid) is the foundation of Islamic education. The second calls for an integrative curriculum that produces a monotheist learner. Finally, the goal of vicegerency (istikhlaaf) unites all sciences under the Islamic framework.

Guided Path
1
Extract the first implication: philosophy of monotheism as foundation
The author states that unity of knowledge includes the philosophy of monotheism, which is a fundamental pillar of Islamic education and the Quranic theory of knowledge. This makes tawhid the starting point.
2
Extract the second implication: integrative curriculum for the monotheist learner
An integrative curriculum should emanate from the idea of unity of knowledge. Its aim is to produce a monotheist (muwahhid) learner, so all content must include the active value of tawhid that unites monotheists across revealed traditions.
3
Connect to the goal of vicegerency
Later in the passage, the author explains that all sciences, when aligned with the Islamic framework, serve the goal of human vicegerency on earth (istikhlaaf). This is the supreme existential and teleological objective.
The author explicitly links this to al-Faruqi and the World Conference on Islamic Education.
4
Note that all sciences become Islamic when aligned with this goal
Sharia sciences meet with other sciences like medicine, engineering, etc., as long as they remain within the Islamic framework and contribute to vicegerency. Thus, any discipline can be Islamic if it intends to serve tawhid and khilafah.
This does not mean changing the methods of science but orienting its purpose.

The Relative Autonomy and Integration of Sciences in an Islamic Framework
Author argues Quranic unity of knowledge from Abrahamic monotheism and derives educational principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why sciences cannot be completely isolated and how al-Faruqi's 'sciences of the ummah' concept resolves the tension between autonomy and integration.

Core Idea
Sciences are independent relatively but connected absolutely through the common goal of vicegerency.

This lesson focuses on the fourth numbered point in the passage, where the author discusses the relative independence of sciences and their necessary integration. The author argues that no science should be reduced to another, yet in the hierarchy of sciences, isolation is impossible. This is resolved by al-Faruqi's notion that social sciences are 'sciences of the ummah' due to their link to khilafah. The passage concludes that all sciences can be Islamic if they serve the goal of vicegerency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim that no science should be reduced to another
The author states it is common to say that a science should not be reduced to another, and social phenomena require social sciences. This acknowledges the distinct methods and domains of different disciplines.
2
Recognize the impossibility of complete isolation
Despite their autonomy, sciences are connected in a hierarchy; no branch can be isolated from the total system of knowledge. Independence is relative, but connection is absolute.
The author contrasts 'relative independence' with 'absolute connection'.
3
Analyze al-Faruqi's argument: social sciences as 'sciences of the ummah'
Al-Faruqi called social sciences 'sciences of the ummah' because they pertain to human vicegerency (khilafah). Since khilafah is social, these sciences are not opposed to natural sciences; both serve a common purpose.
This concept redefines the role of social sciences within an Islamic worldview.
4
Conclude that all sciences can be Islamic when serving vicegerency
The author asserts that sharia sciences and other sciences (medicine, engineering, etc.) meet because all contribute to the goal of human sovereignty over creation, i.e., vicegerency. Therefore, any science can be Islamic if aligned with this goal.
This does not require changing scientific methods but reorienting the purpose and ethical framework.

الباب الثاني: الفصل الأول - أخلاق المعرفة ووحدتها. القرآن أن المسلم يشترك مع جميع دعوات السماء في الإيمان بمبادئهم جميعا. وعندئذ يعلو الناس فوق الانشقاق والتنافس فلا يبقى مبرر منطقي يدفع لشيء من ذلك. وهذا أسلوب القرآن في دعوته الآخرين حتى تنفتح له قلوبهم المتلهفة، لأن الوحدة الأصلية يستجيب لها، ويعتز بها ذوو النفوس السامية جميع. ولهذا فإن الإسلام ورسوله وأتباعه إنما يمثلون امتدادا للملة الإبراهيمية الحنيفية ليس أكثر. وما كان إبْراهِيمُ يَهُودِيًا وَلا نَصْرانِيّاً وَلَكِنْ كانَ حَنِيفاً مُسْلِماً وَما كان مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ، إِنَّ أَوْلَى النَّاسِ بِإِبْرَاهِيمَ لَلَّذِينَ اتَّبَعُوهُ وَهَذَا النَّبِيُّ وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَاللَّهُ وَلِيُّ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ﴾ [آل عمران: ٦٧/٣-٦٨]. وأُمر الرسول بلزوم الملة الحنيفية الإبراهيمية صراحة: ثُمَّ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَيْكَ أَنِ اتَّبِعْ مِلَّةَ إِبْرَاهِيمَ حَنِيفاً وَمَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ [النحل: ١٢٣/١٦]. ويمكن استخلاص المضامين التربوية التالية في ضوء ما تقدّم: ۱ - إنّ وحدة المعرفة تتضمن في أساسها التربوي فلسفة الوحدانية، التي هي ركيزة أساسية من ركائز التربية الإسلامية بوجه عام، ونظرية المعرفة في القرآن بوجه خاص. ٢ - بناء منهج تكاملي انبثاقا من فكرة وحدة المعرفة. -- يهدف المنهج إلى إخراج المتعلّم الموحد، وعليه فإن مفردات المحتوى يجب أن تشمل قيمة التوحيد بصورته الفاعلة الجامعة لطوائف الموحدين من أتباع معارف الوحي. ٤- لقد أصبح من المألوف القول بأن علمًا ما لا ينبغي اختزاله إلى علم آخر، وإن الظواهر الاجتماعية ينبغي تفسيرها بواسطة العلوم الاجتماعية لا بعلوم أخرى. ومن جهة أخرى يبدو من المستحيل في التسلسل الهرمي للعلوم وخاصة اليوم، عزل أي فرع من فروع العلم عن النظام الكلي للمعرفة العلمية. فاستقلال أيّ علم هو استقلال نسبي. وتبدو الصلة بين العلوم مطلقة، ولو أن طبيعة وتركيب هذه الصلة تتغير من حال لأخرى. إن تكامل العلوم على هذا النحو - Chapter Two: First Section - Ethics of Knowledge and its Unity. The Quran that the Muslim shares with all heavenly calls in belief in their principles all. And then people rise above schism and competition, so no logical justification remains to drive any of that. And this is the Quran's method in calling others so that their eager hearts open to it, because the original unity is responded to by it, and those with noble souls all take pride in it. And for this, Islam, its Messenger, and its followers only represent an extension of the Abrahamic monotheistic creed, nothing more. And Abraham was neither a Jew nor a Christian but was a monotheist Muslim, and he was not of the polytheists. Indeed, the nearest of people to Abraham are those who followed him and this Prophet and those who believed, and Allah is the protector of the believers. [Al Imran 3:67-68]. And the Messenger was commanded to adhere to the Abrahamic monotheistic creed explicitly: Then We revealed to you to follow the creed of Abraham, a monotheist, and he was not of the polytheists. [An-Nahl 16:123]. And the following educational implications can be derived in light of what preceded: 1 - That the unity of knowledge includes in its educational foundation the philosophy of monotheism, which is a fundamental pillar of Islamic education in general, and the theory of knowledge in the Quran in particular. 2 - Building an integrative curriculum emanating from the idea of unity of knowledge. The curriculum aims to produce the monotheist learner, and therefore the content items must include the value of monotheism in its active form uniting the groups of monotheists from followers of the knowledge of revelation. 4 - It has become common to say that a science should not be reduced to another science, and that social phenomena should be explained by social sciences, not by other sciences. On the other hand, it seems impossible in the hierarchy of sciences, especially today, to isolate any branch of science from the total system of scientific knowledge. So the independence of any science is relative. And the connection between sciences appears absolute, even though the nature and structure of this connection changes from one case to another. This integration of sciences - including natural sciences - prompted Professor Ismail al-Faruqi to call social sciences 'sciences of the ummah' because they pertain to human khilafah (vicegerency). And if this khilafah is social in essence, then these sciences become 'sciences of the ummah' because they are not in conflict with natural sciences. This affirms the unity of the educational goal. If the goal of Islamic education is nurturing the righteous worshipper - according to the recommendations of the first World Conference on Islamic Education - then sharia sciences meet with other sciences like medicine, engineering, mathematics, psychology, sociology, etc., for all are Islamic sciences as long as they remain within the Islamic framework, commitment and harmony with its particular concept and perception, due to the connection of both fields to achieving the goal of human sovereignty over things and living beings on earth, i.e., the goal of vicegerency as a supreme existential and teleological goal. Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition on the unity of knowledge in the Quran and its educational implications. The author begins by stating that the Quran requires the Muslim to share in the principles of all heavenly (divinely revealed) calls, thereby elevating people above division and competition. This is presented as the Quran's method of inviting others, appealing to their eager hearts because they respond to original unity (fitrah). The author then grounds this unity in the Abrahamic tradition, citing two Quranic verses: one stating Abraham was neither Jew nor Christian but a monotheist (hanif) Muslim, and another commanding the Prophet to follow Abraham's creed. The conclusion is that Islam and its followers are merely an extension of Abraham's monotheistic path. From this, the author derives educational implications in a numbered list. The first implication is that the unity of knowledge encompasses the philosophy of monotheism (tawhid), which is a core pillar of Islamic education and the Quranic theory of knowledge. The second calls for building an integrative curriculum emanating from the idea of unity of knowledge, aimed at producing a monotheist learner; thus, curriculum content must include tawhid as an active, unifying value for all monotheists following revealed knowledge. The fourth point (point 3 is absent) discusses the relative independence of sciences: no science should be reduced to another, and social phenomena require social sciences, yet in the hierarchy of sciences, no branch can be isolated from the total system of scientific knowledge. This integration led Isma'il al-Faruqi to term social sciences 'sciences of the ummah' because they relate to human vicegerency (khilafah). If vicegerency is social, these sciences are not opposed to natural sciences. The author concludes that this affirms the unity of the educational goal: if Islamic education aims to nurture the righteous worshipper, then all sciences (sharia, medicine, engineering, etc.) are Islamic if they remain within the Islamic framework and contribute to human sovereignty over creation, i.e., the goal of vicegerency as a supreme existential objective. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence about science as material of knowledge and method of thinking.

Key Terms
وحدة المعرفة (wahdat al-ma'rifa)
الاستخلاف (al-istikhlaaf)
علوم الأمة (ulum al-ummah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they are easier to learn than natural sciences.
B) Because they are related to human vicegerency (khilafah).
C) Because they are opposed to natural sciences.
D) Because they are purely religious.

Key Takeaway
Sciences are relatively autonomous but absolutely connected through the common goal of vicegerency; any science becomes Islamic when it serves this purpose.

The Unity of Knowledge in Islamic Education
Discusses the relative independence and absolute connection of sciences, advocating integrated knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why all branches of knowledge are considered Islamic in Islamic education and how the concept of vicegerency integrates them.

Core Idea
All knowledge is one in Islam because its source, Creator, and purpose are one.

In today's academic world, disciplines are often sharply separated. The Islamic tradition offers a different vision: all sciences, whether religious or worldly, are intrinsically connected. This lesson explores the Islamic rationale for the unity of knowledge, drawing on the concepts of relative independence, absolute connection, and humanity's role as vicegerent (khalifa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The passage begins by acknowledging the common notion that each science is irreducible, but counters that in the hierarchy of sciences, no branch can be completely isolated. Recognize the tension between specialization and integration.
2
Understand relative independence and absolute connection
Each science has its own domain (relative independence) but is connected to the total system of scientific knowledge (absolute connection). This is a key epistemological principle underlying integration.
3
Explore al-Faruqi's 'sciences of the ummah'
The social sciences are called 'sciences of the ummah' because they are related to humanity's vicegerency (istikhlaaf). This concept ties them to the Islamic purpose, making them Islamic sciences.
4
Link to the goal of Islamic education
If Islamic education aims to nurture the righteous worshiper and achieve vicegerency, then all fields (religious, medicine, engineering, etc.) are Islamic as long as they align with the Islamic framework and serve that goal.
5
Conclude with the holistic view
The unity of knowledge stems from the oneness of the Creator, man, and the universe. All fields of knowledge are one unit in Islam, with no distinction between religious and worldly sciences as knowledge or method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقد أصبح من المألوف القول بأن علمًا ما لا ينبغي اختزاله إلى علم آخر، وإن الظواهر الاجتماعية ينبغي تفسيرها بواسطة العلوم الاجتماعية لا بعلوم أخرى. ومن جهة أخرى يبدو من المستحيل في التسلسل الهرمي للعلوم وخاصة اليوم، عزل أي فرع من فروع العلم عن النظام الكلي للمعرفة العلمية. فاستقلال أيّ علم هو استقلال نسبي. وتبدو الصلة بين العلوم مطلقة، ولو أن طبيعة وتركيب هذه الصلة تتغير من حال لأخرى. إن تكامل العلوم على هذا النحو - بما في ذلك العلوم الطبيعية - دفع بالأستاذ إسماعيل الفاروقي لأن يطلق على العلوم الاجتماعية (علوم الأمة) لأنها متعلقة بخلافة الإنسان. وإذا كانت هذه الخلافة اجتماعية في جوهرها فإن هذه العلوم تصبح (علوم أمة) لأنها غير متعارضة مع العلوم الطبيعية. ويؤكد هذا وحدة الهدف التربوي، فإذا كان هدف التعليم الإسلامي هي تنشئة الإنسان العابد الصالح - بحسب توصيات المؤتمر العالمي الأول للتعليم الإسلامي - فإن علوم الشريعة تلتقي مع بقيّة العلوم من طب وهندسة ورياضيات وعلم نفس واجتماع... إلخ إذ الكل علوم إسلامية ما دامت في الإطار الإسلامي التزاما وانسجاما مع المفهوم والتصور الخاص بـه، وذلك لارتباط المجالين كليهما بتحقيق هدف سيادة الإنسان على الأشياء والأحياء في الأرض، أي هدف الاستخلاف باعتباره هدفًا وجوديًا أسمى وغائيا. وإذا كان المتفق عليه حديثًا أن العلم مادة بناء المعرفة، وطريقة التفكير والبحث، أي إنه يجمع بين الجانب المعرفي والجانب السلوكي، فإن وحدة المعرفة في التربية الإسلامية منبثقة من نظرة الإسلام الكلية إلى الإنسان والكون والحياة. فحقول المعرفة جميعاً وحدة واحدة في نظر التربية الإسلامية، لا فرق بين علوم كونية وأخرى شرعية كمعرفة وطريقة في البحث والتفكير، إذ إن أصلها واحد، والخالق واحد، والإنسان واحد، والكون واحد، فهي تمثل الوحدة الموضوعية في النظرة إلى المعرفة.
It has become commonplace to say that a science should not be reduced to another science, and that social phenomena should be explained by social sciences, not other sciences. On the other hand, it seems impossible in the hierarchy of sciences, especially today, to isolate any branch of science from the total system of scientific knowledge. So the independence of any science is relative. And the connection between sciences appears absolute, even though the nature and composition of this connection changes from one situation to another. The integration of sciences in this way - including the natural sciences - led Professor Ismail al-Faruqi to call the social sciences 'sciences of the ummah' because they are related to the vicegerency of man. And if this vicegerency is social in its essence, then these sciences become 'sciences of the ummah' because they are not in conflict with the natural sciences. And this confirms the unity of the educational goal: if the goal of Islamic education is to nurture the righteous worshiper - according to the recommendations of the First World Conference on Islamic Education - then the religious sciences meet with the rest of the sciences, such as medicine, engineering, mathematics, psychology, sociology, etc., for all are Islamic sciences as long as they are within the Islamic framework, in commitment and harmony with its particular concept and vision. This is because both fields are linked to achieving the goal of man's sovereignty over things and living beings on earth, i.e., the goal of vicegerency as a supreme existential and teleological goal. And if it is agreed upon recently that science is the material for building knowledge, the method of thinking and research, i.e., it combines the cognitive and behavioral aspects, then the unity of knowledge in Islamic education stems from Islam's holistic view of man, the universe, and life. All fields of knowledge are one unit in the view of Islamic education, with no difference between cosmic and religious sciences as knowledge and method of research and thinking, for their origin is one, the Creator is one, man is one, and the universe is one; thus they represent the objective unity in the view of knowledge.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion arguing for the unity and integration of knowledge from an Islamic perspective. It begins by acknowledging the common notion that each science is irreducible to others, but counters that in the hierarchy of sciences, no branch can be completely isolated—independence is relative, and connections are absolute. The author then presents Ismail al-Faruqi's concept of 'sciences of the ummah' for the social sciences, linking them to the vicegerency of man. The passage further emphasizes the unity of the educational goal: if Islamic education aims to nurture the righteous worshiper, then all fields, including medicine and engineering, are Islamic as long as they align with the Islamic framework and serve the overarching goal of vicegerency. Finally, it asserts that the unity of knowledge in Islamic education derives from Islam's holistic view, making all fields of knowledge one unit with a single origin, Creator, man, and universe. Throughout, the author uses examples from Islamic education and sciences to support the argument.

Key Terms
وحدة المعرفة (wahdat al-ma'rifa): The concept that all fields of knowledge are fundamentally one unit, stemming from Islam's holistic view of man, universe, and life, with no distinction between religious and worldly sciences. [Note: In classical Islamic thought, wahdat al-ma'rifa often referred to the unity of divine and acquired knowledge; here it is recontextualized for modern educational integration.]
تكامل العلوم (takamul al-'ulum): The integration of sciences such that each science has relative independence but absolute connection with others, especially within an Islamic framework. [Note: A modern term less common in classical texts; classical discussions focused more on classification than integration.]
الاستخلاف (al-istikhaf): The vicegerency of man on earth, considered the supreme existential and teleological goal linking all sciences to Islamic purpose. [Note: Classical usage is primarily in tafsir and kalam; here it is applied to educational and scientific objectives.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They are completely separate and unrelated.
B) They are both Islamic if they serve the goal of vicegerency and align with the Islamic framework.
C) Only religious sciences are considered Islamic; secular sciences are neutral.
D) Secular sciences are superior because they are practical.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic education, the unity of knowledge is grounded in the holistic Islamic worldview, where all fields, religious and worldly, serve the single purpose of vicegerency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Danger of Dualism and the Necessity of Specialization
Warns against dualism, affirms specialization as necessary but warns against forgetting unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the difference between harmful epistemological dualism and legitimate specialization, and explain why the unity of knowledge must be preserved according to the passage.

Core Idea
Specialization without unity leads to dualism; unity without specialization ignores modern complexity.

This passage confronts a modern educational dilemma: how to reconcile the vast expansion of knowledge with the Islamic imperative of tawhid (unity) in all things. The author warns against a dualistic separation of knowledge into isolated compartments, yet acknowledges that specialization is an objective necessity. The key is to maintain a unifying element that binds all branches of knowledge together, as exemplified by great scholars of Islamic civilization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem of dualism
The passage begins with 'The Danger of Dualism.' Notice that the author is not against specialization per se, but against a worldview that treats different types of knowledge as completely separate and unrelated. This dualism is considered dangerous because it contradicts the holistic Islamic perspective that all knowledge is derived from Allah and should be integrated.
Dualism here refers to epistemological fragmentation, not metaphysical dualism.
2
Recognize the necessity of specialization
The author explicitly states that objective necessity no longer allows for encyclopedic scholars as a general approach. The division of knowledge is a modern necessity due to its continuous branching. This is an honest acknowledgment of practical reality.
The phrase 'فلزوم ما لا يلزم' indicates that expecting everyone to be a universal genius is unreasonable.
3
Understand the need for a unifying element
Despite specialization, the author insists that we must not forget the unifying element (عنصر الوحدة الجامعة) that binds educational systems. This unity is the first step towards wisdom and sagacity (الحنكة والحصافة). The passage then gives historical examples: Hunayn ibn Ishaq (physician), Ibn Sina (medicine and philosophy), al-Kindi (philosophy and natural sciences). These scholars were specialists, yet they worked within the framework of the unity of knowledge.
The examples show that specialization and unity can coexist; it is not an either/or.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خطر الثنائية إن الحديث عن تكامل المعرفة انطلاقاً من وحدتها لا يعني عدم الاعتراف بالتخصصات فذلك - إن فهم - فلزوم ما لا يلزم إذ إن الضرورة الموضوعية لـم تعد تسوّغ وجود طلاب علم موسوعيين أو أشبه بدوائر معارف عصرهم كمنهج عام. فغدا التقسيم ضرورة عصرية في ظل تشعب المعرفة، وتفرعها المستمر. إن تقسيم المعرفة يخدم غاية هامة من حيث تمكين المتعلم من التخصص في حقل ما من حقول المعرفة، ولكنه يجب ألا ننسى عنصر الوحدة الجامعة بين نظم التعليم، لأن ذلك يمثل الخطوة الأولى نحو الحنكة والحصافة. وقديماً عرف تاريخنا وجود متخصصين في إطار وحدة المعرفة كحنين بن إسحاق الطبيب النصراني (ت: ٢٦٠ هـ) الذي عينه المأمون على بيت الحكمة، وابن سينا صاحب كتاب (القانون في الطب) (ت: ٤٢٨هـ)، والكندي (ت: ٢٦٠هـ) و مدار فلسفته الرياضيات والفلسفة الطبيعية)، وغيرهم.
The Danger of Dualism. Talking about the integration of knowledge based on its unity does not mean not recognizing specializations; that would be, if understood, an unnecessary necessary [contradiction]. Objective necessity no longer justifies the existence of encyclopedic students or those resembling encyclopedias of their time as a general approach. Division has become a modern necessity in light of the branching and continuous ramification of knowledge. The division of knowledge serves an important purpose in enabling the learner to specialize in a field of knowledge, but we must not forget the unifying element that binds educational systems, because that represents the first step towards wisdom and sagacity. Historically, our history has known specialists within the framework of the unity of knowledge, such as Hunayn ibn Ishaq, the Christian physician (d. 260 AH) who was appointed by al-Ma'mun over the House of Wisdom, and Ibn Sina, author of 'The Canon of Medicine' (d. 428 AH), and al-Kindi (d. 260 AH) whose philosophy centered on mathematics and natural philosophy, and others.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage addresses the tension between specialization and the unity of knowledge, warning against a dualistic separation. It begins with the title 'The Danger of Dualism' and clarifies that advocating for integrated knowledge does not negate the need for specialization. The author argues that it is unrealistic to demand students be encyclopedic; rather, division of knowledge is a modern necessity due to its vast expansion. However, this specialization must not erase the unifying element that connects all fields of knowledge, which is the first step towards wisdom. The author then provides historical examples from Islamic civilization—Hunayn ibn Ishaq (physician), Ibn Sina (medicine and philosophy), and al-Kindi (philosophy and natural sciences)—to illustrate that specialization existed within a unified framework of knowledge. Thus, the passage maintains that specialization is permissible and necessary, but must be balanced with an awareness of the underlying unity.

Key Terms
الثنائية
التخصص
الحنكة والحصافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Specialization is necessary in modern times, but it must not overshadow the unifying principle of tawhid in knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
